A. Program Purpose and Project Development Objective
1. Program purpose and program phasing:
Mauritania has been engaged for more than a decade in ambitious policy reforms that have affected all
aspects of the country's political, economic and social life. In 1991, a pluralistic democracy was
established with the adoption of a constitution that guaranteed basic liberties and the separation of
executive, legislative and judicial powers, and introduced the practice of universal suffrage for the election
of the President of the Republic and of the peoples' representatives at the National Assembly. The program
of economic reforms, begun in 1992, redirected the Government toward its essential mandate of regulation
and achieved significant progress toward the adjustment of the macro-economic framework, liberalization
of market prices, improvement of the legal and judiciary enviromnent for business activities, and
development of the private sector. At the same time, the country continued its rapid urbanization process,
with urban population from less than 4% in 1962 to the current estimate of 61%, with more than 67% of
the urban population concentrated in Nouakchott (about 612,000 inhabitants) and Nouadhibou (about
76,000 inhabitants).
As a result of these policies, Mauritania has achieved an economic growth rate of 4.4% between 1992 and
1999. Despite this good performance, the poverty situation remains worrisome with about one household
in two still living in poverty. The Government is, however, strongly committed to taking immediate steps to
reduce poverty, as indicated in the Poverty Reduction Strategy Paper (PRSP). The four main themes of the
Poverty Reduction Strategy, which was presented to the IDA Board in January 2001, are: (i) accelerating
economic growth, (ii) implementing targeted public investment programs to reduce inequities and improve
the resource base of the poor, (iii) developing human resources and access to essential infrastructure, and
(iv) promoting true institutional development, based upon good governance and full participation of all
parties involved in poverty alleviation.
During the past decade, Mauritania has made considerable effort to improve the overall level of education
and skills of its population, especially that of women. With the support of several IDA-financed education
projects, coupled with efforts from other development agencies, the Government has expanded educational
opportunities in primary and secondary education and in vocational and technical training. As a result, the
total gross enrollment ratio at the primary level increased from 46.8% in 1990/91 to 86% in 1998/99.
Enrollment at the secondary level also increased by 56 percent from 35,221 in 1990/91 to 60,069 in
1998/99, with girls representing 41 percent of the total. The number of students in technical education
doubled from 880 to 1,682 with girls representing about one third of the total. Similar enrollment trends
have been observed in higher education institutions, where the number of students increased from 7,527 in
1990 to about 13,000 in 1999. Political will to improve education achievements, particularly those of girls
and women, has been the main driving force behind those achievements.
Notwithstanding these remarkable quantitative achievements, there are serious concerns about the quality
and efficiency of the education system.
*   The mastery of curricula content and acquisition of basic concepts and skills in primary and secondary
education is low due to outdated curricula and teaching methods; science education in secondary
schools is poor; and the exposure of the vast majority of students to foreign languages is low,
especially the French language. In fact, while French is widely used in society and essential for highly
qualified jobs, it is taught effectively only to the 5.5% of the students in primary education who enroll
in the bilingual stream. The rest of the students enroll either in the Arabic stream (94%) or in the local
language stream (0.5%), which impart poor French skills.
*   Admission rates at the primary level are extremely high (93%) but retention is low (55%) caused by
high repetition and dropout rates.
-3 -



*   Public resource allocation to education, as a portion of GDP, has been declining significantly, from
5.2% in 1985 to 3.5% in 1998, suggesting that the expansion of the system has been financed through
a steady decline of the unit cost.
*   The technical, vocational, and higher education systems are not sufficiently diversified and lack
flexibility. The unemployment rate among the higher education graduates is estimated at 30%.

To consolidate the quantitative achievements of the past 15 years and to tackle the above-mentioned issues,
the Government of Mauritania embarked on an education sector reform. In 1999, Parliament approved the
education reforn law (the Reforn) which seeks to improve the quality of education and restructure and
expand the education system in ways that are relevant to the present and future needs of the economy.
Specifically, the Reform aims at: (i) combining the existing three education streams into one unified
system, where Arabic and French are the main languages of instruction; (ii) reinforcing the teaching of
foreign languages by introducing French in second grade in primary education and English in first grade in
lower-secondary education; (iii) introducing civic education instruction as a separate subject from 'Islamic
studies' at the primary and secondary levels; (iv) lengthening the duration of lower secondary schooling to
prepare for the provision of 10 years of basic education; and (v) putting greater emphasis on scientific
subjects and information technology in secondary education, particularly by adding physics as a new
subject in lower-secondary, and by introducing new courses to develop computer skills. A detailed
discussion of the Reform objectives and their implications for sector development can be found in Annex
13.
-4 -



Further to promulgating the education reform law, the Government embarked on the preparation of a new
10-year Education Sector Development Program (ESDP), whose scope covers the formal education system
from preschool to tertiary and non-formal education and literacy. The program seeks to: (i) make
education more relevant by using French and Arabic as languages of instruction, (ii) provide improved
access to 10 years of good-quality education (basic education) for the vast majority of the young
Mauritanians while achieving equity, and (iii) make secondary education, technical and vocational training
and higher education more relevant to social needs and responsive to the demands of the labor market.
Improving the quality of an entire education system is a long-term undertaking which requires the main
stakeholders and managers of the system to periodically review progress and adjust courses. The proposed
program provides support to the ESDP in 2 phases, lasting 4 and 5 years respectively. The first or
"foundation" phase is essentially an intensive development phase which will establish structures, tools, and
skills necessary for meaningful expansion and consolidation during the second phase. Specifically, more
school places will be provided in primary education, the quality of education will be improved through the
implementation of the Reform in primary education, innovative programs to enhance the effectiveness of
technical, vocational and higher education will be piloted, and a sound institutional framework will be
developed. The second or "expansion" phase will extend the implementation of the reform to general
secondary education, and scale up the pilot programs in the technical, vocational and higher education
systems and a decentralized administration will be operational following the reinforcement of the
institutional and management capacity in the regions.
2. Project development objective: (see Annex 1)
The proposed credit will help the Government implement phase I of the ESDP, which aims at: (i)
implementing bilingual education at the primary and secondary levels of education, (ii) improving access
and equity, (iii) enhancing quality and increasing the intemal efficiency of the education system, and (iv)
improving the relevance and extemal efficiency of the vocational, technical, and higher education systems.
Expected outcomes include: universal access to primary education, increased completion rates, expanded
access to secondary education, and improved quality of education at all levels.
In order to achieve these objectives the program will focus on four major areas namely: (i) coverage: basic
education access to all, particularly the disadvantaged, girls, and children in hard to reach communities; (ii)
quality of the teaching and learning in schools -- basic physical infrastructure, adequately trained teachers,
and adequate supply of essential didactic materials; (iii) strengthening capacity for institutional and
organizational change - development of leadership and management skills for the design and
implementation of basic sector policies and strategies; and (iv) establishing an effective management
system -- pedagogic, administrative and financial management -- with clear management responsibilities
and procedures.
3. Key performance indicators: (see Annex 1)
The ESDP has indicators for each project component and each sub-sector. The baseline and target values
for these indicators as well as their definition and data collection responsibilities will be detailed in the
Project Implementation Plan. Major outcome indicators, agreed upon with the MEN, to be achieved by
2005 and 2010 include the following:
Quality
i. Average achievement of learning objectives by primary education students to increase from 33%
- 5 -



in 1999 to 45% and 70% in 2005 and 2010, respectively. This indicator measures improvement in
learning achievement in primary education.
ii. Success rate in the basic education completion exam (BEPC, grade 9) to improve from 60% to
65% and 75% in 2005 and 2010, respectively. This indicator is a measure of the quality of
education in the first cycle of secondary education.
iii. Grade repetition in primary education to drop from 15% to 9% and 7% in 2005 and 2010,
respectively. This indicator measures the intemal efficiency of primary education.
Access and Equity
i. Percentage of primary schools offering 6 grades to increase from 17.5% to 45% and 100% in
2005 and 2010, respectively. This indicator measures progress toward the implementation of an
essential strategy to increase the number of students who complete primary education.
ii. Retention rate in primary education to improve from 55% to 67% and 78% in 2005 and 2010,
respectively. This indicator measures the percentage of students that start school and complete
primary education.
iii. Girls will represent 49% of secondary education enrollment (first cycle) in 2005, against 43% in
1999. This indicator measures gender equity in secondary education (gender difference in primary
education enrollment is already low).
Relevance and Effectiveness
i. At least 500 students to be enrolled in apprenticeship schemes in 2005 and 2,000 in 2010. This
indicator measures the relevance of vocational and technical training courses in relationship to the
needs of the economy.
ii. At least 15% of students in higher education to be enrolled in professionnally-oriented courses,
developed in consultation with enterprises, by 2005. This indicator measures the extent to which
the university system is adapted to labor market needs. The target for 2005 has been calculated
with the assumption that the physical facilities of the university will have been improved by that
date.
Financing and Management Capacity
i. The MEN operating budget, as a share of all state revenues, to increase from 13.5% in 1999 to
14.4% in 2005 and 15.5% in 2010. These percentages do not include other ministerial
expenditures on education and training activities. They measure the extent to which the
Govemment is sustaining a stipulated level of education financing in line with the program
requirements.
ii. The correlation rate between the number of teachers and the number of students per primary
school to improve from 79% to 85% in 2005. This indicator measures progress in improving the
MEN capacity to allocate teachers to schools in an efficient manner.
B. Strategic Context
1. Sector-related Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) goal supported by the project: (see Annex 1)
Document number: CAS Report No. 16595           Date of latest CAS discussion: June 17, 1997
A CAS was discussed on June 17, 1997. A CAS Progress Report was prepared in November 1998. The
1997 CAS identifies weak human resource development as one of the major constraints to economic growth
- 6 -



in Mauritania. The strategy lists the improvement of human capital and employment creation as major
areas of intervention. The five main conditions necessary for the achievement of these goals include: (i)
achieving universal primary enrollment, (ii) improving the quality of primary, secondary and higher
education, (iii) strengthening job-oriented vocational training, (iv) encouraging private sector participation
in financing; and (v) delivery of training services and promoting efficiency in the allocation and use of
resources. The objectives proposed in the ESDP are all congruent with the strategies identified in the CAS,
particularly the improvement of education quality, the expansion of access to education, and the
strengthening ofjob-oriented technical and vocational training. The reform and deconcentration of public
adrninistration and services to local Government constitute another key element in the CAS and
Government strategy, and are fully supported by the ESDP.
The proposed program is also designed to achieve lasting results in the fight against poverty, and is a key
pillar of the Government's Poverty Reduction Strategy Paper (PRSP, IDA/SecM2001-0024 discussed by
the Board on February 6, 2001). In rural areas, where 76% of the poor live, the poverty assessment
showed that low income levels are correlated with low labor productivity, which are in turn determined to a
large extent by poor education status. In urban areas, the vast majority of workers from poor households
have no training, and most workers are completely unskilled even in the modem handicraft sector.
Educational opportunities are currently too scarce to improve this situation: 66% of young people lack any
formal training and 45% still leave the education system without completing primary education. By
fighting illiteracy and contributing to the development of a skilled workforce the proposed program will
play a key role in reaching the objectives associated with the main themes of the Poverty Reduction
Strategy Document, which are (i) accelerating economic growth and (ii) developing human resources and
improving access to essential infrastructure.
2. Main sector issues and Government strategy:
Overview of the system. The formal education system operated by the Ministry of Education consists of
six years of primary schooling (enseignementfondamental), three years of lower general secondary
education (college), and another three years of higher-general secondary education (Iycee). Instruction is
provided in three separate streams: (l)fiflire Arabe, (2)fiere bilingue, and (3) a very small stream that
uses local languages and which represents less than 0.5% of primary enrollment. Until 1999 it has proved
difficult for the Government to manage the quantitative and qualitative challenges posed by these multiple
systems and to define a coherent system. As a consequence of the implementation of the reform of 1999,
these streams are being merged gradually. The reform also seeks to implement a 10-year basic education
system by adding a fourth year to lower-secondary education in 2007.
Secondary technical education is provided by two-year programs offered after lower-secondary in
vocational schools, under the responsibility of various ministries, and in technical colleges and lycees,
under the responsibility of the Ministry of Education (MEN). Higher education is offered at the University
of Nouakchott and in five post-secondary specialized institutions. Primary teachers are trained at two
teacher training colleges, Ecoles Normales d'Instituteurs (ENI, in Nouakchott and Aioun); secondary
teachers are trained at the Ecole Normale Superieure (ENS, Nouakchott) which is associated with the
University of Nouakchott.
Results from several diagnostic studies undertaken between 1997 and 2000 have helped the Government of
Mauritania to identify the main issues that its education system faces. The key sectoral issues include: (i)
poor quality of education resulting in weak teaching and learning outcomes and low internal efficiency; (ii)
inadequate coverage resulting in limited and inequitable access to basic education; (iii) inadequate and
inefficient utilization of existing resources; (iv) weak institutional capacity for effective pedagogic,
- 7 -



administrative and financial management; (v) low external efficiency in higher education; and (vi) low
responsiveness of technical education and vocational training to the needs of the labor market.
Poor quality of education and weak learning outcomes. Poor quality of education limits the number of
qualified students who successfully graduate from each level of the sector. An assessment of pupil
performance during the recent sector study revealed that students at the primary level master on average
33% of the intended curriculum content. This national average conceals wide disparities that exist between
students in the worst performing schools mastering 10% of the curriculum content, against 80% in the best
performing schools. Overall performance at the end of the first cycle of secondary is much better with over
half of the students performing well but there are also wide gender differences. In 1998/99, the success rate
at the BEPC examination was 61% for males and 55% for females. Similar gender differences exist in the
results at the end of second cycle examinations. Baccalaureat results were extremely poor, with only 21%
for males and 10% for females obtaining pass grades. These poor outcomes derive from several factors
which include: (i) insufficient and variable supply of textbooks and teachers' guides in schools, (ii) the
absence of other essential didactic materials and equipment, (iii) inadequate pre-service teacher education
and insufficient provision for in-service teacher training, and (iv) ineffective supervision and pedagogic
support.
Government strategy is to revamp the entire learning environment in schools. Facilities that are in
disrepair will be rehabilitated and furnished. Teachers will be well trained both through
professionalized pre-service teacher education courses and demand-driven in-service teacher training
courses. More pedagogic support and guidance will be provided and teachers will be encouraged to be
innovative in their classroom practices. Feedback on students' examination performance will be
promoted to increase pedagogic effectiveness. To facilitate learning, the Government plans to use more
efficient distribution methods to ensure that sufficient quantities of textbooks in the core subjects are
available in all classrooms.
Low retention rates and inequitable access. Despite a relatively high admission rate of 93% into the first
grade, barely 55% of children complete the cycle of primary education. This level is also characterized by
high dropout and an average repetition rate of 15%. Wide gender and zonal disparities in retention rates
exist. For example in rural areas, the retention rate in primary education (percentage of students who
complete grade 6) is estimated at 43%, against 70% in urban areas. It is even lower for rural girls, among
whom only 3 8% reach grade 6. An in-depth analysis of the sector reveals that although there are demand
side factors that may constrain survival at school, the situation in Mauritania is mainly attributed to supply
factors. The major issue is the inadequate supply of school places caused by a high proportion of
incomplete schools. Approximately 83% of primary schools have an incomplete cycle, of which 60%
provide only two grades and the majority of these schools are in rural areas. This makes it difficult for
many children to continue their education once they reach the last grade offered by their school. For social
reasons, this situation has more negative consequences on girls who are less likely to attend and complete
schools than boys.
Government strategy. The Government's priority is to increase the number of school places by
building new schools. The majority of new schools will be complete and located in under-served rural
areas. Existing classrooms will be utilized more efficiently through the promotion of multigrade
teaching especially in areas where the school age population density is low, and will be retrofitted with
latrines and drinkable water. The Government will also increase the number of qualified bilingual
teachers who will cater to the needs of expanding enrollments.
- 8 -



Inefficient administrative and pedagogical management. Although administrative structures exist at the
regional level, management of the education system is still overly centralized. Regional education offices
depend heavily on the central Ministry for directives regarding personnel and staffing matters, preparation
of budgets and resource allocation. Management ineffectiveness also derives from weak resource
allocation and utilization procedures. There are severe weaknesses in the allocation of financial and human
resources from the center to the school level. The teacher posting system which is controlled from the
central Ministry causes considerable inequities between regions, districts and schools. The recent education
country status report noted that the allocation of funds and teachers to individual schools is characterized
by a high degree of variability (graph below). For instance, a school that has 4 teachers can vary in size
from 50 students to as many as 350 students. Administrative ineffectiveness is compounded by shortage of
personnel with management skills at all levels of the system. This has resulted in a weak capacity for
policy formulation and planning as well as financial management at the central, regional and district levels.
Management efficiency is also undermined by the absence of a clear monitoring and evaluation system.
Consequently there is very little or no room for feedback and adjustments regarding the implementation of
education policies and programs.
Government strategy. The Government plans to establish improved administrative and management
structures with clearly defined roles at the center, regional and district levels in order to bring authority
and responsibility for educational service delivery closer to schools and communities. The teacher
posting system will be rationalized and schools will have access to resources for school quality
enhancement programs. An education management infornation system (EMIS) will be established
and the capacity of staff at the central, regional and district levels to efficiently collect, analyze and
utilize data for policy and planning purposes will be reinforced. Training for educational and financial
management will be provided to officials at all levels who will be responsible for carrying out
administrative and financial functions. Guidelines for evaluating, monitoring and reporting system
performance will be developed.

School Size
Poorly adapted technical and vocational training. Since 1992, the Government has made efforts to
improve and expand the supply of technical and vocational training. During this period, student enrollment
-9-



increased from 1,426 to 2,439 and the number of teachers from 184 to 285. Despite this important growth,
the TVT has several serious deficiencies. First, technical and vocational training is poorly adapted to labor
market demands. Mauritania's labor market is characterized by the existence of a traditional and informal
sector, which absorbs the vast majority of the labor supply, and a modem sector which has a limited
absorptive capacity. Nevertheless, technical and vocational training activities, which in their majority are
publicly funded and provided, are geared essentially towards the needs of the modem sector. Second, the
technical education and vocational training services are not well adapted to the needs of the labor market.
The system is limited by the absence of reliable labor market information. Labor statistics which are
collected only for the modem sector conceal the real basis for analyzing employment trends, labor mobility
and the identification of skills shortages. Moreover, there is very little information on the external
efficiency of training centers which in turn makes planning and further resource allocation both from public
and private sources difficult. Third, there is weak coordination between technical and vocational training
and labor market institutions. Traditionally, TVT institutions are placed under the responsibility of too
many ministries, with no clear coordination mechanism among them.
Government Strategy. The Government intends to improve the quality and efficiency of the technical
and vocational education sector by improving links between the institutions and the labor market,
expanding training for the informal sector, increasing the participation of the private sector in financing
and managing training programs. The Government also intends to undertake a number of studies on
employment trends and opportunities as well as the training needs of private sector employers and
artisans. Revision of training programs will be based on the results obtained from these studies. To
strengthen coordination the Government intends to create an institution that will coordinate training
with employment and take the responsibility for policy analysis and relations with employers.
Low external efficiency in higher education. The higher education system was developed in the early
70s, with the creation of the Ecole National d'Administration and Ecole Nationale Sup&rieure, which
trained many of the country's civil servants. The University of Nouakchott was created in 1981 and has
expanded since then with the addition of several new departments and institutes, offering education in the
arts, humanities, social sciences, science and technology. The University of Nouakchott is placed under the
responsibility of the Ministry of Education, but there are a few institutes which are placed under the
responsibility of other ministries. The development of this higher education system, combined with the
training of a significant number of students in foreign universities, has generated a substantial supply of
highly skilled workers, estimated at more than 7,000 persons during the last decade. In 2000, Mauritania
had 12,912 students in higher education inside the country and another 2,439 students on scholarship
abroad. Intemational comparisons suggest that the higher education system seems over-developed in
relation to the size of the country's economy and labor market. Moreover, the supply of higher education
has insufficient professional content and lacks diversity. Only 9% of university students were enrolled in
the science and technology streams in the University of Nouakchott in 1998-99. The combined effect of
these factors lead to low external efficiency, as evidenced by the high unemployment rate among graduates,
around 30% according to the latest surveys.
Government Strategy. The Govenmment plans to revise the existing legal and institutional framework
for higher education rendering them autonomous and capable of raising funds from public and private
sources to finance the services they offer. Courses offered will be diversified and tailored to the needs
of the labor market. The quality of teaching and research will be improved by revising the content of
training courses, providing the necessary infrastructure, equipment and learning materials needed, and
promoting investment in research. Special emphasis will be placed on the promotion of teaching and
learning science and technology.
- 10-



The role of the private education system. Another factor impacting on adequate school space, is the
limited number of private learning institutions in the country. They cover only 2.5% of the total primary
and 6.5% of secondary enrollments. The development of private education has been slow, and limited for
the most part to Nouakchott and other urban centers such as Kiffa, Rosso, Nouadhibou, Timbedra and
Zoueiratt. Limited access by operators to investment funds and appropriate land for the construction of
schools, and the lack of qualified teachers have been the main reasons behind the slow development of the
private sector. Moreover, the legal framework regulating the establishment of new private institutions,
their control by the state, and their taxation regime is outdated and incomplete. Notwithstanding
affordability issues of private education, the willingness to enroll in private institutions appears to be high
in Mauritania. This is primarily because of the perceived management style and quality difference between
public and private institutions.
Government strategy. The Government strategy is to promote private education through the provision
of grants and incentives to private investors. The regulatory framework will be updated and clarified.
Other key Strategies:
The implementation of the education reform of 1999 ranks very high on the Government's social and
economic agenda. To that effect, a comprehensive policy framework and a detailed action plan has been
developed as part of the ESDP program, with clear targets for the next decade. The implementation of the
reform is being phased in one year at a time, and already covers grade 3. It will cover grade 6 in 2004/05,
grade 10 in 2007/08, and the last grade of secondary education in 2010/11. The Government has a
long-term objective to provide 10 years of universal basic education. The technical studies show that while
it is possible to achieve universal primary education, to expand significantly the first cycle of secondary
education and improve quality and equity throughout the system, a universal 10-year basic education is not
achievable during the current decade due to major capacity, financial, and human constraints.
These strategic orientations have been spelled out in the PRSP and in the ESDP documents. A letter of
sector policy (Annex 11), develops the essential policies that support these strategies which have been
discussed with IDA and various donors contributing to the ESDP. The main pillars of the education sector
strategy are as follows:
*   Government Priorities and sequencing of the reforms. The provision of 10 years of good-quality
basic education was given priority in Government investments because of its important role, pointed
out in the PRSP, in poverty reduction. Therefore, a major financial effort will be made during the next
five years in order to provide the facilities and operational budgets necessary to achieve this objective.
As a result, public investments in the second cycle of secondary education, higher education and TVT
aim essentially at improving quality and relevance, rather than expanding access. During that period,
the emphasis will be put on the diversification of training programs and improved linkages with the
labor market, with a view to expanding access when the efficiency and effectiveness of the system has
been improved. The success of this strategy depends on an effective regulation of the flow of students
in the system, which will be accomplished through the separation between the certification exams at the
end of primary and lower-secondary education (CEF and BEPC, respectively) and the access exams to
subsequent cycles. The Government has developed detailed enrollment targets at each sub-level of the
education system, based on alternative scenarios of quantitative growth and their cost implications.
*   The institutional reforms. To implement the reforms, the Government is aware of the necessity of
major institutional changes, aiming particularly at: (i) increasing participation of parents in primary
- 11 -



and general secondary education, and of the private sector in higher education and in the TVT system;
(ii) increasing accountability at all levels through careful monitoring of performance; and (iii)
reorganizing and deconcentrating the administration of the education system to improve its efficiency.
The major actions envisaged to that effect are the creation of school boards composed of
representatives of parents associations and local officials of the MEN, granting greater academic and
financial autonomy to the university of Nouakchott, the creation of consultative processes with the
private sector and demand-driven funding mechanisms in TVT, and the reinforcement of the role of the
Regional Directions of the MEN (DREN) in the process of planning and managing resources.
*   Government financing Plan. Great attention has been paid to the sustainability of the government
financial effort, with a detailed long-term financial plan worked out early in the process and approved
by the Government. The general orientations of this financing plan are as follows (i) all the recurrent
expenditures of the education system will be financed by Government budgetary resources (including
HIPC funds), while the investment effort will be shared with intemational financing partners and, to a
lesser extent, with domestic partners, particularly the communities (see below); (ii) a significant share
of the budget, up to 20% by 2005, will be allocated to non-salary expenditures to provide a permanent
allocation of funds for in-service training and maintenance activities, and for the procurement of small
equipment for the schools and the administration of the education system; and (iii) the teachers'
compensation system will be reviewed to allow moderate general salary increases, enable the allocation
of salary premiums to teachers in remote or difficult areas, and link compensation to performance.
Financial simulations of the ESDP policies suggest that the sustainability of the public expenditures on
education requires that the share of education in GDP should increase from 3.7% in 1999 to 4.7% in
2010. This can be achieved by increasing Govermment expenditures on education from 13.5% of total
revenues in 1999 to 15.5% in 2010. The major multilateral and bilateral donors have been informed
and consulted extensively on this financial plan. In order to avoid overlaps and uncoordinated
interventions, the Government and the major donors have decided to include their interventions within
the framework of the ESDP, with the assistance of a coordination mechanism which is developed in
section C4.
*   The role of Communities and of the private sector. Traditionally, the communities in Mauritania
contribute 30% to the cost of the construction of primary schools in rural areas, in a scheme that has
been in effect and operated successfully by the MEN for more than a decade. This scheme has helped
ensure better ownership of schools by conmmunities, fostered communication between the parents and
the MEN officials and better maintenance of facilities. It has also been a major ingredient in the
planning tools used by the MEN, as it allows for direct appreciation of parents' commitment and the
actual demand for education. During the implementation of the ESDP, the Government strategy will
consist in extending the role of communities to pre-schooling, by encouraging the setting-up of
community-operated programs. In primary and secondary education parents will be encouraged to
participate in school management by participating in school boards. The private education sector is
expected to play a major role, particularly at those levels of the system where the demand might exceed
the available capacity, particularly in upper-secondary, TVT and higher education. The Government
strategy consists in developing an administrative capacity in charge of monitoring the activities of the
private education sector, developing adequate legislation and procedures to regulate the sector, as well
as financial incentives to promote private investment in education. The Govemment has expressed its
determination to conduct a number of consultations during the first and second year of the program,
with the representatives of the profession, to investigate innovative financing mechanisms, including
demand-side and contract funding mechanisms, removing restrictions on private school entry and
sensitizing commercial banks to risk-taking in the education sector. A fund for the development of
- 12 -



private education will be set up after this initial consultation period, which will lead to the
determination of effective methods and detailed procedures for public support to private institutions.
3. Sector issues to be addressed by the project and strategic choices:
The ESDP is based on a sector conceptual framework that comprises all the sub-sectors ranging from early
childhood through primary, secondary technical and vocational to higher education. The program will
address the issues discussed above, but because of the broad-spectrum of tasks to be undertaken in each
subsector, a number of strategic choices had to be made with respect to policy and operational options.
Expansion of secondary and higher education. Given the high enrollments in primary education and
the objective to increase retention rates at this level, a decision had to be made regarding the possibility
of expanding secondary and higher education accordingly or to moderate their expansion until universal
primary education had been achieved. The choice was made to moderately expand access to secondary
and higher education whilst focusing on improving internal efficiency through the enhancement of
educational quality at all levels of the system.
Availability of core textbooks in the classroom. Recent studies have shown that textbooks are a
rarity in primary classrooms due to the high costs incurred by parents and leakages in distribution
methods. Therefore, a decision had to be made on whether to distribute the books free of charge to the
schools or to let parents continue to pay for the books from the market. Given the importance of
textbooks in the learning process and the level of poverty that inhibits parents from buying the books
from the market, the Government decided to distribute the textbooks of the four core subjects at the
primary and secondary level, free of charge, to the schools which will be responsible for their
maintenance.
Duration of pre-service teacher education and supply of qualified bilingual teachers. A decision
had to be made regarding the duration of pre-service teacher education for primary teachers (one year)
and inspectors (four years). It was decided that the duration of pre-service teacher education should be
increased to two years, adding courses in bilingual and pedagogic skills necessary for effective
classroom practice. It was also decided that, due to the high costs of pre-service education for
inspectors, this program should be reduced from four to two years.
Primary scbool construction. It was decided that the community approach to school construction,
successfully used over the past five years, would be continued. This approach reduces cost and
promotes local ownership of the schools by the communities. The possibility of using contractors for
school construction was also considered, but it was rejected because of difficulties in attracting
contractors to remote rural areas and the resulting high costs.
Donor coordination. The Government of Mauritania seeks to lead and organize all donors to work in
partnership amongst themselves and with the Government towards achieving the objectives of the
reform. Mauritania receives significant support from external development partners. However, past
efforts made within the context of education projects by various development agencies have remained
largely uncoordinated and often overlap.
4. Program description and performance triggers for subsequent loans:
The national ESDP has 2 phases, lasting 4 and 5 years respectively. The APL provides support to the
ESDP in 2 phases, lasting 4 and 5 years respectively. During the first phase, more school places will be
- 13 -



provided, the implementation of the Reform in primary education will be completed, and the quality of
education will be improved. To enhance student learning, school performance enhancement programs will
be piloted, a sound institutional framework will be developed, the planning and management capacity
within the Ministry of Education at national, regional and local levels will be strengthened, and new
training modes in technical, vocational and higher education, which will be more relevant to labor market
needs will be introduced. The second or "expansion"phase will extend the implementation of the Reform to
general secondary education and scale up the pilot school performance enhancement programs. Innovation
programs introduced in the technical, vocational, and higher education systems will be expanded.
Decentralized administration will be operational following the reinforcement of the institutional and
management capacity in the regions.
Triggers for Phase 2 were chosen to measure progress towards implementing the Reform, creating an
effective management system, and strengthening the institutional environment of the education system.
Performance triggers for Phase 2 and verification means are as follows:
i. Sustained Government commitment to the ESDP. This will be verified by adequate budget
allocations to the education sector and a distribution within sub-sectors that reflects stated
priorities. The ESDP macro-financial framework (Annex 12) provides the essential data to assess
compliance with this trigger;
ii. Implementation of the Reform on schedule. By the end of Phase 1, the new curricula and
language of instruction will have been implemented in all 6 grades of primary education.
Moreover, the reform should be ready for implementation in secondary education: curricula for the
first grade of the first cycle of secondary education prepared, teachers retrained and textbooks
printed and ready for distribution before the start of the 2004-05 school year;
iii. Institutional and regulatory framework reinforced. Key aspects of this reinforcement are as
follows: (a) the grant system for the development of private education will have been put in place,
(b) a directive reinforcing the university financial and academic autonomy will have been
promulgated, (c) a national textbook publishing strategy, allowing private sector participation in
textbook publishing and distribution will have been adopted by the Government;
iv. Strengthening of the MEN management and administrative capacity completed. In terms of
structure and organization, the new organizational chart of the MEN will have been promulgated
and the regional directorates (DREN) created. In terms of management and administrative
performance, an independent study should confirm that: (a) transparent and efficient teacher and
resource allocation mechanisms exist, (b) clear work procedures and capable human resources are
available at both the central and regional level, and (c) the Direction de la Planification et de la
Cooperation (DPC) and the Direction des Affaires Administratives et Financieres (DAFM) of the
MEN are capable of performing the major project implementation responsibilities that are currently
performed by the Direction des Projets Education et Formation (DPEF) of the Ministry of
Economic Affairs and Development (MAED);
v. Phase 1 implemented successfully. Disbursement performance will have been rated satisfactory
by IDA and at least 7 performance indicators met.
vi. Program for Phase 2 ready. A detailed action plan and financing framework for Phase 2 will
have been prepared and endorsed by both the stakeholders and financing partners.
The program is structured in a way that Phase I would achieve by itself a substantial impact in terms of
reaching universal primary education, implementing the reform, and reinforcing the institutional capacity of
the education sector. Given the Government's high level of commitment to the program and the education
sector's good performance record, the probability that performance triggers will be met before the closing
date are considered high. However, in order to address implementation problems at an early stage, it was
- 14 -



agreed during project negotiations that the mid-term review will assess progress toward: (i) the
promulgation of the MEN's new organizational chart, (ii) the development of a strategy for textbook
development and distribution, and (iii) the implementation of the support grants systems to be financed by
the IDA credit.
C. Program and Project Description Summary
1. Project components (see Annex 2 for a detailed description and Annex 3 for a detailed cost
breakdown):
The ESDP is a sector-wide program that consists of four major components: (i) improving the quality and
relevance of teaching-learning processes and outcomes at all levels of the education sector, (ii) promoting
greater access and regional and gender equity, especially at the primary and secondary levels, (iii)
increasing the extemal efficiency of the education system by fostering closer linkages between technical &
professional training, higher education and labor market demands, and (iv) enhancing the administrative,
technical, pedagogical, and financial management capacity of the individuals and the system as a whole.
These four sub-components are closely linked and mutually reinforcing. (See Annex 2 for details).

Component 1. To Improve the Quality and Internal Efficiency of Education the ESDP will support
the following sub-components:
Sub-component 1-1: Improving the Quality of Teaching-Learning Contents, Processes and Outputs in
Primary Education
a.     Support for the provision of pre-service training activities for teachers.
b.    Support for the provision of in-service training activities for teachers, inspectors and pedagogic
advisors.
c.    Evaluation and dissemination of the new competency-based curriculum.
d.    Provision of a minimum package of teaching and learning materials.
e.    Development of a learning assessment system.
Sub-component 1-2: Improving the Quality and Internal Efficiency of Secondary Education
a.    Development of new curricula, competency-based and in line with the reform objectives.
b.     Support for the provision of pre-service training.
c.    Support for the provision of in-service training.
d.    Development and distribution of textbooks and equipment for laboratories.
e.    Development of the monitoring and supervision system.
Sub-component 1-3: Promoting ECD Activities to Enhance Physical, Socio-emotional and Cognitive
Development of Children
a.    Training of ECD personnel.
b.    Management Capacity Building for SECF (Secr6tariat d'Etata4 la Condition Feminine).
c.    Promotion of Community- and Private-ECD Initiatives.
Sub-component 1-4: Promoting School Health
a.    Promoting good practice of hygiene and nutrition at school-level.
b.    Promoting preventive health care at school-level.
Component 2. To Promote Access to- and Equity in Education, the ESDP will support:
Sub-component 2-1: Promoting Universal Access to Primary Education
a.    Improving planning capacity.
b.    Building classrooms for complete schools and retrofitting schools with latrines and water.
c.    Providing incentives for teachers who work in remote or difficult areas.
Sub-component 2-2: Increasing Access to and Equity in Secondary Education
a.    Reinforcement of planning capacity.
b.    Rehabilitation of classrooms and latrines, expand colleges and lycees and build 30 new small-scale
colleges.

Sub-component 2-3: Promoting the Private Sector Involvement in Education.
a.    Establishing an appropriate institutional and legal framework for the private education sector.
b.    Implementing a grant fund to support private schools.
Sub-component 2-4: Promoting Functional Literacy
a.    Developing the capacity of the SEAEO (Secretariat d'Etat a l'Alphabetisation et a l'Enseignement
Originel).
b.    Promoting the transition of students between the Mahadras and the formal education system.
C.    Promoting functional literacy programs, operated by NGOs and communities and adapted to local
needs.
Sub-component 2-5: Promoting Girls Education
a.    Continuing public awareness campaigns
b.    Piloting small-scale colleges and communitiy-based accomodations to promote access of girls in
rural areas
c.    Conducting a study to identify causes of girls' non-attendance and dropout in low-enrollment
regions.

Education Systems
Sub-component 3-1: Making Technical and Vocational Training More Responsive to Market Demands
a.    Establishmnent of a technical and vocational training fund (TVTF).
b     Reinforcing institutional framework for technical and vocational training.
C.    Building the capacity of the technical education and vocational training centers.
Sub-component 3-2: Improving the Quality and Efficiency of Higher Education
a.    Enhancing the financial and academic autonomy of higher education institutions.
b.    Rationalizing public funding for higher education.
c.    Improving science and technology teaching and research, and implementing a grant fund for
research.
d.    Professionalizing and diversifying training courses.

Education Sector
Sub-component 4-1: Developing Overall Monitoring Structure.
a.    Development of an Educational Management Information System.
b.    Reinforcement of Planning and School Mapping Procedures.
Sub-component 4-2: Improving the Management of Human Resources in the Education Sector.

a.     Creation of a human resources directorate and upgrading of the managerial skills of its personnel.
