2. Key policy and institutional reforms supported by the project:
Early childhood development
The Government's strategy for ECD emphasizes the development of new community-based pre-school
programs and their reinforcement through a network of resource centers, which seek to develop ECD staff
competencies and disseminate best practices. This strategy will lean on the experience of UNICEF in this
domain and will be implemented on a pilot basis during phase 1 of the ESDP.
Primary education
The investment in school facilities will focus on adding on to existing schools where needed to provide six
grades in each school (ecoles completes) and in retrofitting older schools with water and sanitation
facilities in as many schools as possible, based on a case-by-case assessment of availability of water
resources. The proportion of schools that provide 6 grades will be increased from 35% to 59% in 2005,
and to 100% in 2010 in order to accomodate expected retained students. In sparsely populated areas this
will require an extensive use of multigrade teaching.
The investment in quality of education will focus primarily on pre-service and in-service training of
teachers to develop their capacity to operate in a truly bilingual education system. The present teaching
body consists mainly of teachers who are proficient either in Arabic or French. The number of
sufficiently bilingual teachers as well as French teachers in the system is relatively small compared to the
numbers required. Extra effort must be taken to provide intensive language training to current teachers
who have the potential of becoming bilingual, and current pre-service teacher education programs are being
revised and lengthened to include language skills training. In-service teacher courses are also being
strengthened to support the introduction of the recently developed competency-based curricula. A second
important investment in quality relates to: (i) reduction of the average class size from 50 to 36; (ii) increase
in non-salary expenditures, as a ratio of total expenditures from 15% to 20% in 2005 and 23% in 2010;
this increase will cover the minimum package of school inputs, including, materials, maintenance and
teacher training; (iii) establishment of a quality monitoring system that promotes schools' awareness of their
own performance; and (iv) focus on under-performing schools to help them develop school improvement
projects that will be implemented by their school boards. A school improvement grant system will be put in
place to finance these targeted interventions. Key features of the strategy to enhance quality in primary
- 18 -



education are developed in Annex 14.
The textbook production and delivery systems will be subject to a new set of policies consisting in: (i)
organizing the free distribution in primary education of a set of 4 essential free textbooks per student for
classroom use, (ii) implementing a policy of small school libraries, and (iii) liberalizing textbook
development and distribution, with gradual involvement of the private sector in both publishing and
distribution. A national textbook publishing policy will be developed before the end of 2003 and will be
implemented before the end of phase 1 of the ESDP.
Secondary education
The transition rate from primary to secondary will remain unchanged at 50%. As a result of the expected
increase of the retention rate of primary education, the number of new students accessing secondary
education will increase steadily from 18,300 at present to 35,000 in 2010. In order to promote the access
of girls and students from rural areas, the Government is committed to building small-scale colleges in
small and medium-size towns, and to draw rural students to these colleges by encouraging communities
and parents to develop and operate appropriate accommodations. As regards the second cycle of secondary
education, the number of students will stabilize at around 14,500, which will enable the implementation of
proposed quality-enhancing measures.
The quality of secondary education will be enhanced through: (i) the renovation of the curricula, with
greater emphasis on science education and on foreign languages, (ii) the development of in-service training
programs to accompany the language and curricula reform, (iii) the provision of textbooks and laboratory
equipment, and (iv) the reduction of the average class-size from 50 to 37 in lower-secondary, and from 42
to 38 in upper-secondary. These measures are expected to enable a reduction of repetition rates from 13%
to 11% in 2005, for the first cycle, and from 12% to 6%, for the second cycle. Key features of the
Government strategy to enhance quality in secondary education are developed in Annex 14.
To address the gap between girls and boys, which is still significant nationwide, the Government has
signed a cooperation agreement with several UTN organizations (UNICEF, VNDP, UNFPA and WFP) to
conduct a pilot program in the regions of Assaba, Brakna, le Gorgol and the Guidimagha. The purpose of
this program is to determine a model of intervention, including the provision of appropriate accommodation
and the implementation of information campaigns, that will be extended nationwide in 3 years, once the
pilot phase is completed.
Technical and Vocational Training
In the TVT sub-sector, the Government's objective is to successfully adapt the training system to the
specific qualitative and quantitative needs of all production sectors, including the informal and service
sectors of the economy. This will be achieved through the implementation of institutional reforms that aim
at making the provision of TVT demand-driven. The main aspects of the reform are: (i) establishment of a
new autonomous institution, the INP-FTP, with a bipartite board, which will have broad responsibilities
over the coordination of TVT activities, the provision of technical support to the training institutions to
adapt their training supply and the financing of new demand-driven training activities; (ii) improvement of
the quality and availability of information on skill requirements of enterprises; (iii) analyses of the major
trends in the evolution of the content and needs of qualifications; (iv) development of more flexible training
modes, based primarily on apprenticeship and school-to-job schemes; (v) monitoring of the insertion of
graduates to jobs; and (vi) the development of school financing and management mechanisms based on
- 19-



performance. Given that these reforms require an in-depth reorganization of the institutional framework,
they will be phased in gradually, on the basis of a pilot project in a limited numnber of sectors, which will be
taken to scale in the second phase of the program.
Higher Education
In higher education, the Government strategy consists of the following points: (i) renovation of the
institutional framework which reinforces the autonomy of the institutions while creating a strong planning,
monitoring and coordination mechanism in the MEN; (ii) diversification of the supply of training,
enhancement of the job-related content of university courses, particularly that of short (2-3 years)
diplomas; (iii) improvement of the quality of teaching and learning; (iv) rationalization of the sector
expenditures, especially through a gradual reduction of expenditures on scholarships and their replacement
by pedagogical activities; (v) reinforcement of the teaching and research capacity by promoting staff
exchanges with foreign universities, providing incentives and funding for applied research, and promoting
the use of the new information technologies; and (vi) the reduction of the student teacher ratios from 31 to
14 in 2010. These objectives will be reached over a decade, with the first phase being dedicated to the
institutional changes, the adaptation of the legal framework, and the piloting of new professionally-oriented
training courses. The Government also envisages the construction of a new integrated campus in
Nouakchott, including teaching facilities, a central library, a university restaurant and accommnodations for
students to be operated by the private sector under contract with the university and to rehabilitate and equip
the science and technology institute in Rosso. These investments aim to create an adequate environment for
teaching, research as well as other aspects of university life. The successful implementation of this plan
requires that during this transition period the intake of new students be limited to 2,600 a year, and the total
number of students to about 7,500.
Literacy
'The Government seeks to: (i) achieve greater complementarity between the formal education system and
the traditional system (Mahadras), and (ii) promote functional literacy through a new type of adult literacy
program. To allow the Mahadras system to contribute fully to the national education goals, the
Govermment will put in place an incentive system enabling the Mahadras to benefit from State funding and
pedagogical support if they commit to complement their teaching with subjects that will help their students
move to the formal education system, if they desire to do so. The Government will also promote the
provision of functional literacy and non-formal education programs for adults and unschooled and
out-of-school youth to be implemented by outsourcing of program development and delivery to NGOs and
other components of the civil society.
School health
A variety of actions aimed at enhancing the health of school-aged children will be implemented on a pilot
basis, including the emphasis on hygiene, the construction of latrines, provision of drinking water,
dewormiing and the distribution of micro-nutrients to eradicate the deficiencies in iodine, iron, and vitamin
A in several affected areas that have been identified in health surveys.
Management and Administration
The reinforcement of the MEN capacity to plan and manage education development is one of the most
important project objectives. A concrete preparatory step has already been taken through the completion of
an audit of the MEN structure and organization. This audit provided a detailed assessment of the
- 20 -



structural and organizational deficiencies of the MEN and helped develop a reorganization plan through a
participative process involving all staff. The main structural actions that will be undertaken to reinforce
the institutional capacity of the MEN are as follows: (i) revision of the MEN organizational chart at the
central level, (ii) creation of Regional Directorates (DREN) to deconcentrate the central administration and
clarification of the responsibilities at each level, (iii) appointment at the central and regional levels of
qualified managers on the basis ofjob profiles and competencies that will be defined prior to recruitment,
and (iv) improved coordination mechanisms between the ministries of education, health, vocational training
and other ministries involved in vocational training through formnal administrative links and coordination
arrangements. In addition, the organizational audit will be extended to the Institut Pedagogique National
(IPN), which is under the responsibility of the MEN. These activities will be implemented over the first
phase and, when completed, will provide the MEN with a coherent structure, strong institutional capacities,
and clear procedures.
As a result of the actions above, the MEN's administrative and management performance should improve
significantly during Phase 1. One key result will be to narrow the differences in resource allocation among
regions by rationalizing expenditures. Teacher deployment is especially targeted by this process and will
henceforth be: (i) performed by clearly designated officials who will be accountable for its results at the
central and regional level; (ii) based on a simple set of criteria; and (iii) limited in time to the summer
vacation period. To facilitate appointments in the remote areas, which have a very high teacher turnover
rate, significant incentives will be provided, including a salary premium of 15% that could benefit as many
as 35% of the teachers. The successful policies and activities to promote girls' education will be continued,
aiming at a few provinces where girls' enrollment is still lagging behind that of boys in primary education.
It is expected that these measures will substantially enhance equity throughout the system.
Particular attention will be paid to perfornance monitoring at the level of the schools. This will be
achieved both through the traditional functions of inspectors and school directors, who will be trained and
provided with adequate communication means, and through a more efficient monitoring system that enables
the collection and dissemination of assessment results on a continuous and comparable bases. The
Government will also encourage the development of parents' associations to reinforce the accountability of
the schools vis-a-vis their stakeholders.
Human resource management will be enhanced by a better monitoring of careers, the introduction of a
performance-based merit system, and the provision of incentives to encourage the conversion of Arabic
teachers to bilingual teachers, which provides much needed flexibility in the staffing of smaller schools.
3. Benefits and target population:
Benefits:
The program will benefit approximately one million primary, secondary, technical and vocational and
higher education students over a period of ten years. Primary and secondary school students will have
access to more places in better school facilities, improved supply of learning materials and better teaching
methods. The number of primary school children projected to be admnitted into grade 1 will increase by
about 48% from 66,275 in 1998 to 98,427 in 2010. Within the same period, the number of students
admitted into first grade of first cycle secondary will almost double from 18,344 to 35,000. Approximately
13,650 primary and 3,749 secondary teachers will also benefit from increased pedagogic support and
training. Overall program outcomes are linked to Mauritania's poverty reduction strategy, which highlights
the development of human resources and access to essential infrastructure as one of its main themes. The
Government program is expected to have the following impacts:
- 21 -



Economic - Improved quality of life. Improved access, equity and quality of schooling will result in
increased enrollment in primary and the first cycle of secondary education and in higher completion rates.
Graduates will be better prepared in general life skills, including improved health practices. Vocational and
technical education trainees will demonstrate improved entrepreneurial skills which will enhance
productivity, labor market mobility, employment prospects, an increase in lifetime eamings, and economic
growth. The quality of life, particularly for girls, will be improved through the reduction of fertility,
maternal and infant mortality rates. Improved nutrition and health standards will increase the likelihood for
children of beneficiaries to receive better education.
Social - Reduced inequalities. The provision of school places and adequate learning materials will
increase access and, in particular, benefit children in rural, poor, and disadvantaged areas, thereby reducing
inherent imbalances in educational provision in the country. Girls will also benefit from a gender-sensitive
strategy applied to all areas of educational provision. By targeting training and development needs of
youth and workers through the technical and vocational training system, the program will increase
productivity in the labor market. This will in turn improve equity, reduce social tensions linked to
unemployment, and contribute to the alleviation of urban poverty.
Institutional - Improved service delivery. The program will strengthen the key strategic functions of the
Ministry of Education and put in place efficient procedures for the allocation and use of financial and
human resources. The Government will administer public education more efficiently while encouraging
privately provided and financed educational institutions as well. The decentralization of education
management from the center to the regions and districts will lead to more effective delivery of educational
services, in which communities, local providers, and parents will be more involved.
Target population:
The program will target: (a) pre-school children aged 5 and under; (b) children presently in primary
schools receiving poor quality education; (c) out-of-school children of school-going age, particularly girls
and children in poor rural and disadvantaged areas, who will have better educational opportunities;
(d) students at the secondary level who have access to quality education that prepares them for a productive
professional future; (e) teachers who will benefit from training and increased professionalism; (f) adults
and drop-outs who will participate in and benefit from literacy programs; (g) youth between the ages of 15
and 25 whose skills will be improved through technical and vocational education and whose productivity in
the labor market will increase; (h) students in higher education who will be adequately prepared for medium
and high-level professions in the labor market; (i) staff of the Ministry of Education at the central,
provincial and district levels; and (j) school directors, teachers and communities who will become more
involved in the decision-making process regarding improvements at the school level.
4. Institutional and implementation arrangements:
Implementation Period: The program is to be implemented over a nine-year period in two phases:
2002-06 and 2006-10.
Implementing Agencies: MEN, the Secretariat d'Etat d la Condition Feminine (SECF), and the
Secretariat d'Etat a l 'Alphabetisation et a l'Enseignement Originel (SEAEO).
Guiding Principles: The ESDP is a sector program implemented by the MEN and two State Secretariats,
with the MEAD playing the coordination role. It encompasses all MEN activities. ESDP implementation
- 22 -



will be based on annual Action Plans and Budgets (APB). Strong donor coordination through joint reviews
will ensure that execution of each donor "project" is consistent with the ESDP. This donor coordination
will lead to a more complete integration of support during Phase 2, where the goal is to implement a
SWAP. Phase 1 of the APL will focus on building MEN management capacity.
Overall implementation arrangements: The MEN will handle primary, secondary, and higher education
and play a coordinating role in vocational training activities between all private and public providers and
beneficiaries. The SECF will take responsibility for Early Childhood Development and the SEAEO will
manage literacy. Each component or sub-component will be implemented by the appropriate Directorate
(see Annex 2). Financial and procurement tasks will be handled by the DPEF within the MAED in Phase 1
and by MEN in Phase 2. The sharing of implementation responsibilities between the MEN and the DPEF
during phase 1 is spelled out in detail in the Letter of Sector Policy (Annex 11).
Coordination of ESDP implementation:
Program Coordination Committee (PCC). The PCC will coordinate implementation of the ESDP,
meeting at least twice a year. After preparation of draft APBs by component/sub-component, and
consolidation / arbitration by the PMC, the PCC will review the overall APB for submission to the
Ministers and donors. The PCC will also monitor perfornance and meet the donors during joint annual
reviews. The PCC will include all central and regional MEN directors; directors of institutions such as
IPN, ENS, and the University; the director in charge of Early Childhood Development in the SECF; the
directors in charge of Literacy and the Mahadras in the SEAEO; the directors of contract management
agencies such as AMEXTIPE and the DH (Direction de l'Hydraulique); private sector
representatives; the director of the budget in the MF and the Directeur de la Programmation et des
Etudes in the MAED; and the director of the Collectivites Locales in the Ministry of Interior.
Program Management Committee (PMC). During Phase 1, the PMC will be composed of the DPC
of the MEN and the DPEF of the MEAD. The DPC is responsible for coordination of MEN
Directorates both intemally and with other Ministries and donors. The DPEF is responsible for extemal
resources and national counterpart funds required under foreign credit and grant agreements.
The Action Plan and Budget (APB) Process
The annual APB is the implementation mechanism for the ESDP, helping the sector to monitor
progress and implement corrective measures:
a. each component manager will review implementation of the previous APB and transmit a new
draft APB to the PMC, after stakeholder consultation;
b. the PMC will (i) prepare a consolidated APB monitoring report and (ii) consolidate all
individual draft APBs into an annual sector-wide APB with adjustments based on financial
resources available;
c. the PCC will assess ESDP performance and validate the proposed APB for the following year;
d. the assessment report and the new APB will be submitted to donors for conunents during the
annual joint review;
e. the MAED, MEN, SECF, MSAS and SEAEO will approve the new APB;
f. the PMC will monitor implementation and ensure coordination with extemal partners. It will
also prepare a mid-term APB review by the PCC.
- 23 -



The following criteria will guide the assessment by the PMC of the annual APB: (i) consistency with
ESDP objectives; (ii) consistency of implementation strategies with the Letter of Sector Policy; (iii)
consistency of student flows for each sub-sector with the macro-financial framework, (iv) consistency
with the ESDP macro-financial framework; (v) consistency with external partner agreements; (vi)
consistency between expenditures to be financed by other internal partners (parents, the private sector,
local communities) and input received from these partners.
Building MEN management capacity. During Phase 1, plarning responsibilities will be handled by MEN
and financial and procurement responsibilities by the DPEF while capacity is built in the relevant MEN
directorates. MEN capacity building will be completed by the end of Phase 1 and MEN will be able to
handle all of its financial and procurement responsibilities under both national and external financing. In
particular, the DPC and DAFM will be able to handle the project implementation responsibilities that are
currently performed by the DPEF.
Financial and disbursement arrangements:
The PMC will prepare the budgets. During the first phase of the program, all donors will make
disbursements within the ESDP framework but under separate credit or grant agreements with the (see
Manuel de Procedures Administratives, Financieres et Comptables):
a. expenditures financed by the budget will be managed according to national guidelines;
b. expenditures financed by some international donors, including IDA and AfDB, will be managed by
the DPEF. This directorate has a strong capacity for the management of such funds and sound
knowledge of donor financial procedures. It will manage, inter alia, the AfDB and IDA special
accounts.
c. expenditures for some bilateral donors, UN agencies and NGOs will be managed by the
organization itself.
Financial responsibility of the DPEF during Phase I of ESDP
During Phase 1, the financial responsibilities of the DPEF will be to: (a) implement the financial MIS,
designed around the APB process; (b) carry out budget planning in relation with donor support, including
coordination of the APB process, for presentation to the MF and development partners; (c) prepare monthly
applications for replenishments of Special Accounts or direct payments by IDA and AfDB and similar
mechanisms for special accounts of other donors; (d) ensure the initial deposit and annual replenishment of
Project Accounts from the BCI for donor-financed projects, including IDA; (e) execute expenditures from
Special Accounts and Project Accounts; (f) monitor expenditures of donors and autonomous institutions
such as the universities; (g) oversee management of construction by AMEXTIPE; (h) maintain and
consolidate all ESDP program accounts, including IDA Special Accounts and special accounts of other
donors; (i) monitor compliance with the Manual of Accounting and Financial Procedures; (j) ensure that
audits are submitted three months prior to annual reviews; (k) coordinate the preparation of quarterly
Program Management Reports (PMR); and (1) prepare the overall financial report for the ESDP, verify its
consistency with the initial financial framework for the ESDP, identify gaps and propose remedies.
Procurement arrangements:
During Phase 1, procurement arrangements will be consistent with the relevant financing agreements with
- 24 -



donors. Arrangements are summarized as follows:
a. procurement of goods and services financed under the recurrent budget will be consistent with
national procurement procedures;
b. procurement of goods and services financed by the Investment Budget (BCI) and by international
donors will be carried out by the DPEF as per agreements with donors;
c. procurement of works - with the exception of primary school construction and wells -- financed by
the Government and external donors will be delegated to AMEXTIPE, for smaller contracts in
Nouakchott and the other towns;
d. procurement of works and goods for primary school construction by the Government, AfDB and
IDA will be delegated by the DPEF to communities, while works for drilling and equipment of
wells will be delegated to DH;
e. procurement of goods, works and services financed by UN agencies, bilaterals and NGOs will be
managed by the organization itself.
A participatory procurement capacity assessment was conducted during program preparation (see Annex 6)
and its results were incorporated into the project.
Donor coordination:
Extemal support is project-based with the ESDP framework. Under DPC-DPEF coordination, the
Government, AfDB, and IDA conducted joint preparation missions, in close collaboration with the French
Cooperation. Preparation of the Mahadras component was also prepared in close collaboration with
UNICEF. With IDB, these 4 donors account for 98% of the external support to ESDP. All ESDP external
partners will be invited by the PMC to jointly participate in annual APB reviews. A draft memorandum of
understanding is under discussion by the donors to be signed at project launch.
Monitoring and Evaluation Arrangements:
ESDP's performances will be monitored with a set of key indicators which are provided by the ESDP
logframe in Annex 1. The PMC will prepare an initial Action Plan and Budget for SY 2001-02 which will
be jointly reviewed in December 2001 during the Launching Workshop which will coincide with the first
joint ESDP Annual Review. ESDP indicators will be broken down by school year and the first set will be
included in the first APB for 2001-02. Subsequent annual Action Plans and Budgets will be prepared for
joint review by the Government and donors in September of each year. During the reviews, the
Govemment and donors will review the progress on the basis of the performance indicators for the relevant
year.
Performance indicators for each component and sub-component will be incorporated into the relevant
modules of the Program Implementation Plan (PIP) to monitor the progress of the various
componentlsub-component in terms of inputs, processes and outputs. The PIP also describes arrangements
and responsibilities for component-level monitoring, reporting and evaluation. The information will be
incorporated into the Education Management Information System (EMIS) to be developed under the
program.
Quarterly program implementation reports carried out by the DPEF will provide accounting and financial
information on the program, including procurement activities and physical progress. An integrated FMIS
will be developed under the program.
-25 -



Implementation capacity:
Smooth implementation will necessitate considerable capacity building in all directorates, especially
regarding the implementation of the reform from Arabic mono-language to Arabic-French bilingual
teaching/learning. In parallel, management capacity building of the MEN is needed for the transfer of full
management responsibilities from MEAD to MEN. Capacity building interventions have been designed on
the basis of: (i) recommendations from the Institutional Audit conducted during the previous project
Education V; and (ii) capacity needs assessment conducted during project preparation whose results are
reflected in the PIP and the COSTAB. These capacity building interventions will be multi-pronged and
include:
a. redefinition of the Terms of Reference of each Directorate and Institution and their managers and
key staff, on the basis of the responsibilities listed in the PIP;
b. adoption of a new MEN organization on the basis of the above mentioned redefinition, including
the creation of a DRH (Direction des Ressources Humaines), and issuance of all related
documents (dcrets and arrit6s);
c. recruitment of new staff (on Government budget) in the DPC and DAFM with adequate skills to
address MEN needs for professional capacity in planning, economic analysis, financial analysis,
personnel management, budgeting, financial and accounting management, and procurement;
d. training of the new staff-- expected to be hired with adequate academic background but probably
limited professional experience -- to enable them to perform their responsibilities. Part of this
training will be provided by proficient long-term technical assistance recruited under contracts
specifying their clear skill-transfer mandate including benchmarks and performance indicators for
this transfer;
e. provision of equipment, tools, and facilities needed by the implementing agencies (DPEF, DPC DP,
DAFM) to carry out their roles and functions under the program.
D. Project Rationale
1. Project alternatives considered and reasons for rejection:
The Government of Mauritania and IDA discussed the option of conventional lending versus an APL,
combined with a sector-wide approach (SWAP) to support the ESDP. The ESDP has essentially been
prepared as a sector-wide program, and the Govemment has announced that donor contributions are
expected to take place within the ESDP framework. However, while recognizing the potential benefits of a
SWAP, the Mauritanian Government had mixed feelings about the seemingly complex coordination and
management procedures that a SWAP entails. A SWAP is currently being implemented in the health sector
in Mauritania and has been disbursing too slowly. Moreover, the MEN's current capacity for strategic
planning, programning and monitoring of public expenditures does not allow for effective donor
coordination. For this to happen, there would need to be: (i) a national system of financial management
and procurement that are demonstrably economical and efficient; and (ii) a willingness of all major
partners to shift from project aid to budget support.
An in-depth discussion of the potential benefits and disadvantages of a SWAP approach in the education
sector has led to the development of arrangements that are appropriate for the current situation in
Mauritania. These arrangements consist in keeping donor contributions independent and under separate
agreements during Phase 1, while maintaining overall coherence by focussing all financial assistance on the
achievement of the ESDP objectives as spelled out in the letter of sectoral policy (Annex 1 1), the 10 year
macro-financial framework (Annex 12) and the implementation and monitoring arrangements (Section C
- 26 -



above). In particular, the monitoring arrangements seek to increase the efficiency and coherence of donor
financial support through the implementation of Annual Budget Plans and joint reviews. At the same time,
the donors agree to firther integrate their financial support during phase 2, when the MEN planning,
financial management and procurement capacities are fully developed.
2. Major related projects financed by the Bank and/or other development agencies (completed,
ongoing and planned).

3. Lessons learned and reflected in the project design:
Lesson 1: Past projects have been limited to sub-sectors where each donor chose what to finance and
improvements were isolated and piecemeal. The Government needs to develop a comprehensive and
coherent strategy for the education sector with coordinated donor interventions.
Response: The Government has developed a coherent strategy for the entire education sector,
undertaken an economic analysis, and estimated the cost of implementing the strategy within the
country's macroeconomic framework. Joint annual reviews of program implementation by the
Government and donors have been included as part of the Government's effort to ensure donor
coordination in the education sector.
Lesson 2: Institutional capacity development in the Ministry of Education has been slow and limited partly
because it has been based on the narrow concept of borrowing skills through technical assistance and
training. Long-term technical assistance experts were often under-utilized and there were no clear plans to
ensure the transfer of knowledge to their Mauritanian counterparts. Training was often ad hoc and not
based on a coherent strategy for sector improvement.
Response: The proposed program addresses these issues by taking a broader, more holistic and
operational perspective of capacity building. It will deal with all the elements of institutional capacity
such as organization, planning, administration, financial, human and material resource improvement
and management. The program will depend on the existing management capacity of the project
coordination unit in the Ministry of Economic Affairs and Development during the first phase, while
the institutional capacity of the Ministry of Education is being strengthened. The technical capacity of
staff at the central, regional and district levels of the Ministry of Education will be developed, and
dependence on extemal technical assistance will then be limited to specific areas of high-level expertise.
Lesson 3: Efficient service delivery and improvements in the quality of the teaching and learning process
depend on management structures that are closer to the schools and an empowered teaching force.
Previous projects have not paid sufficient attention to the decentralization of educational management and
planning or to the empowerment of teachers and school directors to take full responsibility for the
educational outcomes of their schools.
Response: The current program design draws on deconcentration experiences from neighboring
countries, such as Guinea, Senegal and Mali, which have clearly defined the roles of regional and
district structures. It seeks to develop a good education information and management system (EMIS)
and a functional monitoring capacity at all levels. The program will support a pilot school
improvement initiative with the aim of developing management capacity at the school level. The roles
of inspectors in in-service teacher training will be redefined so that they respond to teacher-driven
training initiatives that the program will support.
Lesson 4: The textbook sales approach and the book distribution methods used in past projects, have left
many children, particularly those of the poor, without textbooks. This has seriously impeded effective
- 28 -



teaching and learning in schools and has been one of the causes of internal inefficiency.
Response: The proposed program is designed to ensure that all children at the primary level receive a
set of textbooks in the core subjects. The textbook policy and distribution system will be revised and
schools will be responsible for taking care of the books. Schools will be provided with other didactic
materials that will enhance the teaching and learning process. Teachers will receive sufficient training
in the development of new ways of responding to the wide range of pupils' learning needs.
Lesson 5: The effectiveness of community participation in primary school construction has been
demonstrated in past projects. The approach has promoted local ownership of schools and has encouraged
community financing.
Response: The current program is designed to continue support for community participation in
primary school construction while simplifying the long and tedious bureaucratic procedures for
obtaining financing. The program will also encourage community involvement in decision-making and
the local management of schools through school boards and parent teacher associations.
Lesson 6: Private sector provision of education has been weak at the primary and secondary levels and
does not exist at the level of higher education. Private financing and provision of technical and vocational
education have been limited. For the Government to attain the educational goals it has set, it will need to
stimulate the development of private sector provision and financing for general, technical and vocational
education.
Response: The program is designed to make available a support fimd for the development of private
primary and secondary education in order to attain the objectives of the education sector program. A
training support fund will also be established to fund skills-acquisition programs and improve the
planning and management of technical education and vocational training centers. Links will be
established with employers and information about labor market demand and employment trends will be
collected regularly.
4. indications of borrower commitment and ownership:
Government commitment and ownership has been very strong as demonstrated below:
*  Education is a high priority sector in the national development agenda as indicated by political
pronouncements and budgetary allocations. The proportion of the national recurrent budget
allocated to education will increase from 13.5% to 14.9% in 2005 and 15.5% in 2010. The
Government is committed to meeting these targets and has taken the proactive step of setting up
arrangements for annual monitoring with donors.
*  The ten-year education sector development program and an implementation plan supported by a
rigorous economic analysis were prepared within twelve months, mostly by Mauritanians. The
quality of the project documents produced, along with the decision to appoint young professionals
with the necessary technical expertise to head the directorates of the Ministry of Education, also
shows an ownership of the project that matches the political determination to modernize the
education sector.
*  The Government is committed to implementing tough policy decisions such as limiting the number
of students in the university and focusing on the improvement of quality and increased scientific
research. It also seeks to increase cost recovery and private financing of higher education.
- 29 -



5. Value added of Bank support in this project:
The Bank's knowledge of educational issues in national contexts all over the world, as well as earlier and
current involvement in the education sector in Mauritania make its support to this prograrn highly valuable
to the Government of Mauritania. During program preparation, the Bank provided technical advice on
educational, financial and management issues to the national team. Specifically, Bank support helped in
bringing ideas and experiences from other countries to bear on discussions of policies and strategies. The
Bank is considered to have added value to enhancing the quality of the documents developed, particularly in
its emphasis on preparing a logical framework with key performance and monitoring indicators at the
beginning of program document preparation. The Bank also played a major role in ensuring that a coherent
program with an adequate implementation framework was established.
Throughout program implementation the Bank will play a key role in providing technical advice during
program supervision missions. The Bank will also provide long-term financial support through a
two-phase adaptable program loan (APL) which is particularly valuable given the fiscal constraints and in
light of the major investments needed to achieve the development goals of the sector.
E. Summary Project Analysis (Detailed assessments are in the project file, see Annex 8)
1. Economic (see Annex 4):
O Cost benefit    NPV=USS million; ERR = % (see Annex 4)
* Cost effectiveness
O Other (specify)
The sector study (Le systeme educatif-mauritanien: elements d 'analyse pour instruire des politiques
nouvelles, May 2000) carried out a number of cost-effectiveness analyses to better understand the
relations between school organization structure, availability of educational inputs, and performance of
schools. The study was conducted on a sample 429 schools, from a total number of 2,656 schools. The
sample included a large variety of situations in terms of school endowment, pedagogic organization and
academic performance, enabling the analysis of the relation between several key factors. School
performance was measured by results in the primary education completion exam (CEF). The average
pass rate in the CEF was 37% on average in the sample, with a very high standard deviation (26%).
Analyzing the effect of class-size, student-teacher ratio, single/multi- grade teaching and double shifts, it
appears that double shifting has negative impact on the success rate in the CEF, while multigrade
teaching has no noticeable impact on school performance. On the other hand, both the high student per
teacher ratio and large class size are associated with lower school performance. The average class size
was 47.4 (with a standard deviation of 21) and the average student per teacher ratio was 51.8 (standard
deviation 25) which are both relatively high. A increase of 10 students per class is associated with a 3%
decrease in the pass rate of the CEF exam. Anther noticeable result was the apparent weak impact of
teachers' formal qualifications. The teacher body is very diverse. About 90% of teachers are fully
qualified, 9% partially qualified (instituteurs adjoints) and 1% have no formal qualification. However,
in some schools as many as 20% of the teachers are either partially qualified or unqualified. The
percentage of the variance suggests that the composition of the teacher body had only a marginal effect
on school performance.
Similarly, the examination of the availability of teaching-learning materials and other desirable
characteristics of the school environment show interesting results. It is important to mention that the
school database contains information only on the existence of small school materials (such as a ruler or a
- 30 -



box of small weights), as well as the existence of facilities such as latrines, canteens and playground.
With regard to the availability of small pedagogic materials, only the best endowed schools have
significantly better results. These schools represent 14% of the sample, while 60% of the schools in the
sample have no pedagogic materials at all. Similarly, the results are positively correlated with the
condition of the school building, i.e., the existence of a canteen, water and other facilities. Due to
extreme shortages, textbooks were not part of the package of teaching and learning materials that was
considered. However, separate analyses showed that the existence of textbooks and teacher guides
produce a significant improvement in results.
Despite many data limitations, this analysis draws attention to a few interesting facts. First, many of the
findings are coherent with established research findings. Second, they tend to support the strategies and
policies of the ESDP, particularly the emphasis on teacher in-service training to enhance their language
capability, the provision of pedagogic materials, the retrofitting of schools with latrines and water, the
planned slight decrease of average class size and teacher per student ratios, the use of multigrade
classes, the limited recourse to double shifting and the emphasis on school maintenance.
2. Financial (see Annex 4 and Annex 5):
NPV=US$ million; FRR = % (see Annex 4)
Fiscal Impact:
Detailed projections of expenditures and sources of financing for the 10-year education program were
produced by the Government and are found in Annex 12. These analyses show that, based on widely
accepted assumptions for GDP and revenue growth, the fiscal impact of the ESDP is relatively modest in
comparison with its expected benefits. Key aspects of the financial simulations show that:
* The Government will provide 62% of ESDP expenditures, corresponding to the majority of
operational expenditures in the sector.
*  To adequately fund the ESDP program the Government will need to increase MEN operational
budget from 13.5% of total state revenues to 14.4% and 15.5% in 2005 and 2010, respectively.
Total education sector expenditures, including expenditures by ministries other than MEN, will
increase to 14.9% and 16.8% during the same period.
*  Intra-sectoral allocations will also need to change in percentage of the MEN's budget: the share
allocated to primary will increase from the current 46% to 52%, because of the priority given to
this level, the need to recruit additional teachers and the emphasis on providing teachers and
students with adequate teaching and learning resources. The share of the first cycle of primary
education will also increase from 15% to 19%. However, following the decision to rationalize
access to the second cycle of secondary education and to higher education, and to emphasize
quality and effectiveness over quantity, the share of second cycle secondary education and higher
education in the total education budget will decline from 13% and 18% to 12% and 10%,
respectively, in 2010.
3. Technical:
The program design is appropriate to the needs of the country. Proposed activities are related to
findings from a recent sector study which highlights the main characteristics of the education
system. The major issues identified include limited and inequitable access to primary and secondary
education resulting mostly from supply constraints, poor quality and low intemal efficiency (high
repetition and dropout rates), low institutional capacity for systems management, mninimally
responsive technical and vocational education, inefficient higher education, inadequate funding and
- 31 -



sub-optimal allocation of resources particularly at the school level.
Program objectives and performance targets are based on projections of a simulation model using
assumptions regarding enrollment and repetition rates as well as pupil teacher ratios. An analysis
of the overall level of recurrent costs, including the incremental costs and savings produced by the
program for example when repetition rates are reduced in primary from the current 15% to 7%, and
in secondary from 7% to 3% by 2010, indicates that the program will cost approximately US$320
million. This will be financially viable when the national budget is supplemented by external funds.
Estimates for investment costs are based on current market rates and allowances for physical
contingencies and price increases during implementation have been adequately accounted for.
Technically, program activities conform to educational principles and draws from lessons learnt in
past projects and from sound educational practice elsewhere. The strategies envisaged include:
School construction: To attain universal primary education by 2005 while admitting
approximately 85,000 pupils in the first grade of primary education and ensuring that 78% of them
complete the primary cycle, the Government has decided to ensure that all primary schools provide
a complete cycle by 2010. This will involve the construction of new classrooms and the use of
multigrade teaching especially in the rural areas.
Demand and supply of trained teachers: The Ministry of Education recognizes the constraint of
recruiting sufficient numbers of candidates who meet the requirements for bilingual pre-service
teacher training. It has decided to embark on a two-pronged approach. First, the existing one-year
pre-service teacher education will be increased to two years. The first year of training will be
devoted to language skills and the second year partly to pedagogic matters and supervised teaching
practice in the schools. Second, the existing corps of teachers will be categorized into two groups -
those who have the capability of being bilingual and those who do not. The former group will
undergo intensive language training and the latter will continue as monolingual teachers in the
schools. In addition, the ESDP also emphasizes a dynamic teacher support system where there will
be links between pre-service and in-service teacher training, regular teacher seminars and
pedagogical support to schools from the inspectors.
Pedagogical materials: Presently, the Govenmment relies on the national pedagogical institute
(IPN) for the development and printing of textbooks. The planned transition from state monopoly
to competition in publishing will be phased in gradually within the first phase of the program. The
issue of textbook procurement and distribution has been long-standing and complicated. The
existing policy of cost-recovery for primary school textbooks has seriously limited their availability
in the classroom. The Government has decided to provide free textbooks to students in primary and
secondary schools and to implement a carefully planned transition program to privatization.
Decentralized management of schools: The Government has decided to create regional and
district management structures that will respond faster to the demand and supply issues of the
schools. The program will also support the development of a school improvement model that will
ensure quality and relevance of schooling.
Lengthening of first cycle of secondary education: According to the recent educational reform,
the length of the first cycle of secondary education will be increased from three to four years to
better prepare students for studies either in the second cycle of general education, technical and
vocational education or employment.
- 32 -



Responsiveness of technical and vocational education to labor market needs: The management
of technical and vocational education will be restructured; the quality will be improved and linked
directly to the needs of the labor market. Provision of technical and vocational education will be
expanded to the regions.
External efficiency of higher education: The Government has taken special measures to improve
the performance of higher education by putting in place an institutional framework that increases
the autonomy of higher education institutions and investing in scientific courses and research that
are adapted to the labor market.
4. Institutional:
The choice between a sector approach vs. project approach, which is discussed in section Dl, will have an
important impact on the borrower's capacity to carry out project implemnentation, as well as on the
effectiveness of the cooperation between the Government and its financial partners. An approach based on
the aggregate of individual donor projects combined with strong sector-based management tools such as
APBs and donor coordination was selected to allow the Government to apply its strong capacity in
individual project management while strengthening its sector-based management procedures, and is the
most appropriate choice in order to ensure successful implementation.
4.1 Executing agencies:
The executing agencies are listed in para. C4.
4.2 Project management:
During phase 1 of the ESDP, the program management will be under the responsibility of the (DPEF)
within the MAED. For 25 years the management of education projects has been under the responsibility of
the DPEF, an ad hoc PIU under the responsibility of the Ministry of Economic Affairs and Development
(MAED). The DPEF has a good performance record in project implementation, including procurement and
financial management. Both MEN and MAED have expressed their satisfaction with the current
arrangement because it compensates for MEN's weak management capacity, and wished to keep it
unchanged during phase 1, while MEN's capacity is being built up. Both the MEN and the MAED agree
to phase out the DPEF during phase 2 of the ESDP, when the MEN's management capacity is expected to
allow the management of complex operations.
4.3 Procurement issues:
The national procurement code, and public procurement monitoring is being improved on the basis of the
1999 CPAR with support of an IDF grant. A participatory procurement capacity assessment of the
various agencies involved in procurement (DPEF, DAFM, AMEXTIPE and Communities) conducted
during preparation concluded that the overall risk is average. The DPEF, which has extensive experience
and a good track record, will carry out the main bulk of procurement for goods and services while
construction will be delegated to AMEXITIPE for collges and office buildings, and to communities for
primary schools. An action plan has been deternined for enhancing DPEF procurement capacity to enable
it handle the ESDP workload (see Annex 6). AMEXTIPE procurement capacity will be enhanced under
the parallel Urban Development Project. Procurement capacity of the DAFM is extremely weak. During
the first phase of ESDP, this Directorate will have procurement activities limited to contracts financed by
Government through the MEN's operating budget while full procurement capacity will be built with a view
to transferring DPEF procurement responsibilities in the second phase. Communities have already full
capacity to implement the community-based primary school building program (with DPEF technical
- 33 -



support) as evidenced by its performance record in the implementation of similar programs over the past
ten years.
4.4 Financial management issues:
During the first phase of the ESDP, the DPEF will handle financial management and will maintain the
books and accounts. It will also be responsible for the production of the annual financial statements. The
DPEF have a satisfactory financial and accounting unit, which has gained vast experience in IDA
procedures during previous projects (the General Education Project and the Vocational Training Project).
However, this unit will be reinforced with better computer equipment, a revised procedures manual, and
training. Building financial capacity of the DAFM will be one of the main objectives of the ESDP to allow
DAFM to handle ESDP financial responsibilities by phase 2.
5. Environmental:                 Environmental Category: C (Not Required)
5.1 Summarize the steps undertaken for environmental assessment and EMP preparation (including
consultation and disclosure) and-the significant issues and their treatment emerging from this analysis.
The project was classified under Category C for the following reasons:
Water Supply. Communities living near the Senegal River can build their wells as the water table is not
deep. The population in the rest of the country rely on support from the Ministere de l 'Hydraulique et de
l 'Energie (MHE) because groundwater is deep, requiring drilling equipment. Eligibility criteria for new
school construction include certification of existence of water in the area by the MHE so that no school can
be constructed without available water sources. This arrangement for bore-hole drilling has been effective
since the inception of the last two IDA projects, about 10 years ago, and will be updated under the
proposed program to ensure proper implementation of the school-water component.
Sanitation. The Govermment has developed and tested standard specifications for latrines, taking into
account the social context. The use of latrines is well established among the long-term sedentary
population and hence the use of school latrines will not pose difficulties. As for the nomads and those
recently sedentarized, the Govenmment is in the process of developing workable solutions. In addition to
providing new latrines, the proposed program will also retrofit existing schools with latrines and promote
school hygiene.
Waste water disposal. Waste water disposal is not a problem in the sedentary areas where the population
follow well-established practices. However, the nomads and those recently sedentarized, will have to be
introduced to hygiene practices as the process of sedentarization progresses. Under the school health
component, the Ministry of Health and NGOs will engage in this task to support the Ministry of Education.
Construction material. The communities use cement blocks and corrugated metal roofs as construction
materials since wood and stone are in short supply. The standard design and technical specifications are
embodied in the Agreements between the Ministry of Education and the communities.

6. Social:
6.1 Summarize key social issues relevant to the project objectives, and specify the project's social
development outcomes.
Vulnerable groups. The prograzn targets activities to rural areas and to under-served regions where 75%
of the poor live. Most construction, rehabilitation, and provision of water and latrines will be in these
areas. The deconcentration of responsibilities and the subsequent better allocation of teachers and other
resources among regions will give poor communities equal access to public investments in education.
Finally, the project seeks to reach gender equity in education, with specific activities aimed at increasing
girls access and retention, particularly in secondary education and in low access regions.
Resettlement & land acquisition. The proposed program will not support school construction requiring
the resettlement of the population in Nouakchott and Nouadhibou because this will be done in the context
of the proposed Urban Project which will also deal with related resettlement issues. The remaining urban
areas of the country are not densely populated, and hence, no resettlement issues will arise. Similarly, there
will be no resettlement issues in rural areas. Furthermore, no land acquisition will be necessary in the
urban and rural areas covered by this proposed program.
6.2 Participatory Approach: How are key stakeholders participating in the project?
Not Applicable
6.3 How does the project involve consultations or collaboration with NGOs or other civil society
organizations?
Community participation. Comnmunities are very supportive of the concept of schools construction and
latrines as a package. As in previous IDA projects, they will contribute to classroom construction and
guarantee subsequent maintenance of the facilities. The same arrangements used in the last two projects
(1990-2000), based on state-community partnership and where communities finance 30% of the cost of
classrooom construction will be applied. Some cost sharing will be continued while communities will be
100% financed by the credit for all other facilities.
NGO participation. Although the detailed procedures of the Government support to community-based
ECD programs, functional Literacy programs, and the traditional Mahadras has not yet been worked out, it
is expected that NGOs and communities that are active in these fields will be closely associated during
implementation and are likely to be sub-contracted to implement significant portions of these programs.
6.4 What institutional arrangements have been provided to ensure the project achieves its social
development outcomes?
The project will be implemented by a Project Unit, staffed inter alia with three engineers and several
technicians who will be responsible for the classroom program in the country (each engineer and his team
being responsible for one third of the territory. They will visit the sites to assist communities at the various
stages of construction). The DH of the MHE will be responsible for the provision of water supplies, the
- 35 -



Ministry of Health and NGOs will deal with hygiene education and the provision of deworming pills and
micro-nutrients, and the communities will be responsible for the construction of schools and latrines.
Formal arrangements have been developed between these ministries to assure good coordination. At the
regional level, the project will reinforce inter-regional equity by reinforcing the structure and the
competences of the staff in the regional offices of the MEN and progressively delegating decision-making to
these offices. The new grant mechanism to channel funds to the schools through the SPIG will help to
empower the school boards and enhance ownership of schools by staff and communities.
6.5 How will the project monitor performance in terms of social development outcomes?
The project has detailed performance indicators for each component. These indicators will be updated on a
yearly basis by the Government and discussed with the financial partners during the annual joint reviews.
Moreover, As part of the PRSP process the Government has launched a Living Standard measurement
Survey (LSMS), which has a detailed education component and which will provide a baseline for most
impact indicators. The follow-up LSMS or household surveys that will be implemented as part of the
PRSP monitoring system will provide a useful basis to further assess of project outcomes.

F. Sustainability and Risks
1. Sustainability:
The simulation model confirms that the major determinants of the financial sustainability of the program
are the teachers' compensation system and the transition rates from primary to lower-secondary. The
ESDP program would affect teachers' compensation policies significantly through an increase of 24% of
the average salary in real terms over the next decade, incentives for bilingual teachers, and incentives for
teachers appointed to remote areas. However, the average annual increase in the salary of individual
teachers is slightly lower than the rate of increase of the per capita GDP. As a result, in 2010 the average
teacher salary would represent 4.5 times the per capita GDP, against 5.1 times at present. Despite this
slight relative decrease, international comparisons show that the average teachers' compensation remains
reasonably high.
The transition rates from primary to lower-secondary and then to upper-secondary have been kept
unchanged from their current level of 50%. Yet, the combination of demographic growth and the
- 36 -



significant improvement of the retention rate of primary education would produce, over 10 years, a
substantial increase in the number of new students in the first year of secondary education, from 18,344 to
35,000, and an increase in total enrollment from 35,000 to 92,000. Simulations show that a higher
transition rate would be unrealistic. For example, a transition rate of 70% would imply an increase of the
new entrants to 48,000 per year and of total enrollment to 126,000. Although such an increase would be
desirable to achieve the goal of universal basic education quickly, it would not be feasible on financial
grounds and would almost certainly exceed the limit of the MEN planning and implementation capacities.
Given the policies discussed above, the ESDP would be sustainable with the following assumptions: (i)
realistic economic performance, based on an average annual economic growth of 6% and a rate of fiscal
mobilization of 27.8 %; and (ii) the share of education in total public revenues increasing from 13.5% to
16.5%, and as a share of GDP from 3.7% to 4.6%.

Risk Rating - H (High Risk), S (Substantial Risk), M (Modest Risk), N(Negligible or Low Risk)
The overall risk has been rated substantial, which leads to the development of strong risk-mitigating
policies and arrangements. The lack of information about the labor market, and particularly on
employment and on qualification requirements in the informal sector, is one of the risks considered to be
substantial. While the MEN has no mandate to carry out regular employment surveys (a special agency,
the Institut National des Statistiques, has this mandate), it will nevertheless develop tracer studies of TVT
and higher education graduates to assess their insertion and carry out specific sector studies to assess the
economic viability of the creation of new training institutions or the development of new courses within
existing institutions.
The ESDP's success will depend on the extent to which there will be efficient student flow controls in the
public system. As seen in section Fl, an increase in transition rates from primary to lower-secondary and
from lower-secondary to upper-secondary would put excessive pressure on the system's capacity to provide
high quality education. To mitigate those risks, the Govemment will put in place very strong regulatory
and financial incentives to develop the private education sector. IDA fully supports this policy.
Failure to implement this program successfully, in view of its large scope and MEN's currently limited
capacities, is another substantial risk. To mitigate this risk, the program will rely on the DPEF, which has
a strong implementation record, until the end of Phase 1. Additional risk-mitigating factors are: (i) the
existence of a detailed PIP, prepared through a participatory process to build ownership, (ii) the
commitment of the Govemment to recruit 2 procurement specialists by project effectiveness and 10
managers in the MEN, (iii) the reinforcement of the institutional capacity of the MEN and (iv) explicit
monitoring arrangements with attention to strong donor coordination, which will trigger quick palliative
reactions to identified deficiencies or delays.
Finally, education quality targets might not be reached. It may be difficult to find teachers with a good
mastery of the French language to implement the language of instruction reform. This reform requires a
substantial increase in the number French language teachers in primary education, and of science and math
teachers that can use French. The Govemment strategy tackles this issue from three different angles: (i)
adapting teacher recruitment policy in which permanent employment status will be awarded only to those
among the new recruits that show adequate progress in French, (ii) offering intensive in-service training
courses, as well as salary incentives, to the teachers currently in the system who show an aptitude and
willingness to become bilingual, and (iii) improving teacher deployment to optimize the use of bilingual
teachers who will be appointed as a matter of priority to multigrade classes and smaller schools, while
- 38 -



monolingual teachers will be appointed to larger schools. Moreover, detailed training and retraining
programs are being developed (see Annex 14).
3. Possible Controversial Aspects:
It is expected that social pressure to increase intakes in secondary education, TVT, and higher education
would build up quickly into political pressure, to a point where compliance with the macro-fmancial
framework might weaken. The Government needs to prepare an effective communication campaign and to
continue to involve a wider range of stakeholders in informal discussions about the program. To further
mitigate this risk, the Government would promote private education through an adequate legal and
regulatory framework and by setting up a Private Education Development Fund. International experiencs
shows that when there is an enabling legal and regulatory environment, as well as efficient financial
incentives, the growth of the private education sector is usually rapid and could become effective in
reducing the pressure on public education institutions.

Annex 2: Detailed Project Description
MAURITANIA: Education Sector Development Program
The principal objective of the Education Sector Development Program (ESDP) in Mauritania is to support
the Government's Programme National de Developpement du Secteur Educatif (PNDSE 2001-2010) in
its entirety. The ESDP is a sector-wide program that has four inter-related objectives: (1) to improve the
quality and relevance of teaching-learning processes and outcomes at all levels of the education sector, (2)
to promote greater access and regional and gender equity, especially at the primary and secondary levels,
(3) to increase the efficiency and relevance of the education system by fostering closer linkages between
technical, professional training and higher education, on one hand, and the labor market demands, on the
other hand, and (4) to enhance the administrative, pedagogical, and financial management capacity of the
individuals and the system as a whole. These four themes are closely linked and mutually reinforcing.
Principal Conypneub of tbe Maurilania Ediwatbn Sector Dueqp!nent Program
2Access andEqit                                 3 Efcenc
4. Magemnt
While the major investment of the proposed program will be at the primary level, the program covers all the
sub-sectors in the education sector: early childhood development, primary education, secondary education,
technical and professional training, higher education, school health, literacy, and the management of the
sector. The advantage of this comprehensive approach is to address system-wide issues in a coherent and
coordinated manner, to define clear priorities and to strengthen the linkages between the education sector
and the economy.
The proposed project is an APL with two phases planned from 2001 to 2010, lasting four and 5 years
respectively. The project (ESDP) builds on the long history of the Bank's engagement in the education
sector in Mauritania since 1975, especially the most recent General Education Project (Education V) and
the extensive sector study, including simulation exercises for the 2001-20 10 period, that was carried out in
2000 (Le systeme educatif mauritanien, elements d 'analyse pour instruire des politiques nouvelles) and
which was key in defining the Government 10-year education development program. The proposed ESDP
is therefore an integrated part of this Government program, and aims to support all its components.
-45 -



Focus of Two Phases
The first phase of the ESDP will focus on: (1) implementing the Reform program in primary education and
preparing its implementation in secondary education, (2) piloting new initiatives in vocational and technical
education, higher education, literacy, early childhood development and school health, and evaluating these
experiences, and (3) building the institutional framework and capacity for more effective management in
the education sector. The Second phase will focus on: (1) consolidating the Reform in primary education,
(2) implementation of the Reform in secondary education, (3) expanding and institutionalizing successful
programs in vocational and technical education and higher education, and (4) institutionalizing the new
management framework developed during phase 1 and achieving the deconcentration of the MEN.
