By Component:
Project Component I - US$107.63 million
Component 1. Improving the Quality and Internal Efficiency of Education
Objective
The objective of this component is to promote effective teaching-leaming processes in classrooms and
increase the internal efficiency of the education system by enhancing student achievements, reducing
repetition and dropout rates especially for girls and children in rural underserved areas. This will be
attained by improving bilingual and professional skills of teachers through revised pre-service and
in-service teacher education programs, providing pedagogic support to increase the competence of serving
teachers, providing textbook and other didactic materials, and assessing student performance to monitor the
quality of education provided.
- 46 -



The contributions of various development partners for Component 1 will be the following: IDA $15.6
million, AfDB $2.0 million, Cooperation Fran aise $8.0 million, lUNDAF $0.3 million, Islamic
Development Bank $15.3 million, UNICEF $0.3 million, and UNESCO $0.06 million. The Government
will finance $52.6 million, the local communities $0.07 million, and $12.3 million will be made available
through the HIPC for this component.
Key Indicators
*   Retention rate of primary education (grade 6) is increased from the current 55% to 67% in 2005
and 78% in 2010, and repetition rate is decreased from the current 15% to 9% in 2005 and 5% in
2010 in primary education.
*   Student to teacher ratio at primary level is improved from the current 48/1 to 44/1 in 2005, and
40/1 in 2010.
*   Success rate of the first cycle secondary school diploma (BEPC) is increased from the current 60%
to 65% in 2005, and to 75% in 2010.
*   Students in science stream in secondary education is increased from the current 52% to 65% in
2005.
Subcomponent 1-1: Improving the Oualitv of Teaching-Learning Contents. Processes and Outputs in
Primary Education
Key Strategies and Activities
a. Pre-service Training. The content and duration of existing pre-service teacher education program has
been found to be inadequate in providing primary school teachers and inspectors the professional skills
necessary for articulating school curricula and meeting pupils' learning needs. The ESDP supports the
re-design of pre-service training programs, emphasizing language skill development and more practical
teaching prior to certification. The training course content will be restructured to balance the student
teacher's mastery of content and teaching methodology in accordance with a teacher professional profile
that has recently been developed. Links between teacher training institutions (ENI) and primary schools
will be strengthened to ensure effective supervision and guidance of student teachers during practical
training. The pre-service training duration for teachers and inspectors will be extended from 1 to 2 years
and reduced from 4 to 2 years respectively. The first year which will mostly be college based, will be
devoted mainly to the development of language skills and mastery of curricula content, whilst the second
year will largely be spent practicing in primary schools. During this time student teachers will be
supervised by experienced teachers in the schools and tutors from the training college. Graduate teachers
will be qualified to teach all six grades and will be trained in multigrade teaching techniques in order to
facilitate the establishment of complete six-year primary cycles in all schools and ultimately improve the
utilization of teachers in the schools. It is estimated that approximately 1,300 student teachers and 80
student inspectors will be trained per year during the first phase of the program. To achieve qualitative
improvements in pre-service teacher education, the skills of teaching and administrative staff of training
colleges will be upgraded through intemal and extemal training programs.
(Phase 1) The first phase will focus on establishing a 'new vision' for professional teachers as articulated
in the "Teacher Professional Profile or Referentiel" that was drafted with French technical assistance. The
ESDP will fund activities to revise and apply the profile to teacher preparation and performance at each
career stage. The profile will include the following areas amongst others: knowledge of subject matter;
pedagogical skills; curricula knowledge; conduct, initiative and leadership. Current pre-service and
-47 -



in-service training courses will be mapped against the profile to deduce areas of strengths and weaknesses,
indicating where change is required and guide the development of strategic training modules. Teacher
educators, head teachers and inspectors will be assisted to apply the profile as part of their normal
appraisal procedures of trainees and serving teachers. Guidelines for the use of the profile will be developed
and workshops to refine its optimum use will be organized based on trials and feedback from key
stakeholders (teachers, teacher educators and professional associations). The profile will be used to define
standards for teacher certification. The ESDP will support the development, implementation, monitoring
and evaluation of the new training modules. At the end of their training, newly qualified teachers will each
be given a pedagogic kit comprising textbooks and guides to enable them to settle quickly into their new
classrooms when they take up duty.
(Phase 2) The revised training modules and their implementation at ENI will be evaluated in 2005/2006
with focus on language training and practical/professional training. The same evaluation will be carried
out for inspector training at ENS in 2007/2008. The training modules will be revised based on the
recommendations of the evaluations, to better integrate the subject knowledge, practical and professional
skills, and educational psychology in the training modules.
b. In-service training. The in-service training activities will have three objectives: (i) upgrading the
linguistic skills of the teachers to allow them to perform in a bilingual system, (ii) prepare the introduction
of new competency-based curricula, and (iii) provide continuous professional development to the teachers,
based on needs identification.
(Phase 1) In-service teacher training programs will be strengthened to increase the professional
competence of all primary teachers. Emphasis will be on the acquisition and implementation of practical
skills and the use of instructional materials to strengthen teaching. An action plan for in-service training
based on the identification of teachers' needs will be prepared. In-service training programs will be
organized for primary teachers, school directors, inspectors, pedagogic advisors, and tutors of ENI, with a
special emphasis on upgrading linguistic skills and mastery of curriculum content. To promote innovations
in pedagogy, action research will be conducted.
(Phase 2) The training program will be continued, with an emphasis on professional development. Based
on needs assessment and the recommendations of the evaluation report to be prepared in 2005, the teacher
training modules will be improved. Successful training programs at school levels which have been
developed during phase 1 will be brought to scale.
c. Development and dissemination of the new currlculum. This activity aims at testing and
implementing a new competency-based curriculum, which has been prepared in accordance with the reform
objectives.
(Phase 1) The new curricula will be tested for each grade, implemented and monitored in primary
education, including sensitization and orientation of teachers. An evaluation of the new curriculum - both
intended and implemented curriculum - will be conducted.
(Phase 2) Necessary revisions will be made in the primary education curriculum, based on their evaluation
during Phase 1.
d. Teaching-Learning Materials. This activity seeks to (i) achieve the provision and effective use of a
minimum package including a set of four textbooks per student, small-scale librarie,s and other teaching
- 48 -



and learning materials, (ii) provide a regulatory and institutional framework conducive to more effective
private sector participation in textbook publishing and distribution.
(Phase 1) This activity will procure and distribute free of charge a package of four textbooks for key
subjects (Arabic, French, Mathematics and Science) to all primary education students, for school use.
Alternative methods will be piloted in selected schools, depending upon the local context, and especially the
existence and commitment of a local parents association. This sub-component will also define the
minimum package of teaching-learning materials to be standardized and distributed to schools. Depending
on the size and situation of the schools, school libraries or "reading comer" will be promoted on an
experimental basis. A national textbook publishing and distribution policy will be developed and approved
before the end of 2003.
(Phase 2) The textbook distribution policy will be continued. Based on the evaluation of the activities of
phase 1, the content of textbooks and the teaching-leaming material package as well as their distribution
mechanism will be updated during the second phase. Successful textbook-use experiences will be shared at
regional and national levels. The small school library and reading corner experiences will be taken to scale.
The implementation of the national textbook publishing and distribution policy will be completed.
e. Student Assessment. This activity seeks to (i) develop systems, tools and practices for an effective
assessment of student achievement, (ii) help disseminate the assessment results at the school level and draw
policy lessons, (iii) help low performing schools develop a School Improvement Plan, and (iv) set up a
small grant fund to support the implementation of the School Improvement Plans.
(Phase 1) Both summative and formative student assessment will be reinforced, and teachers will be
trained in the practice of classroom continuous assessment. The selection exam for the access to secondary
education will focus more on key competences
than "memorization" of facts. This will require the training of the personnel involved in the administration
of exam in the design of testing instrument, correction method, and the management of exams. These
activities will be jointly coordinated by the DEF and the examination department of DES, IEF and IGEST.
(Phase 2) A systematic assessment practice will be consolidated in order to support teachers to assess their
student's progress. The school improvement grant system will be expanded.
Subcomponent 1-2: Improving the Oualitv and Internal Efficienev of Secondary Education
Key Strategies and Activities
Quality improvement of secondary education will be achieved through comprehensive and integrated
interventions in: curriculum development, pre- and in-service training, the provision of textbooks, materials
and science laboratories, and improving the working environment of teachers.
a. Development of new curriculum. In accordance with the Reform objectives, the new curriculum for
secondary education, including the curriculum for newly added 4th year of the I st cycle secondary school
(4AS), English from the 1st year (IAS) and the information technology from the 4th year (4AS), will be
developed and existing curriculum will be revised.
(Phase 1) The new curriculum will be experimented, evaluated, validated, and disseminated. Similarly for
the primary curriculum, the approach will be competency-based. The process of curriculum renewal and
- 49 -



development will be used as an opportunity to introduce population and family life education. The
preparation of the new and revised curriculum will take place during the first phase of the ESDP, and will
be introduced starting from the academic year 2004/05 to meet the implementation schedule of the reform
program.
(Phase 2) The implementation of the curricula will be continued in accordance to the implementation
schedule of the reform program, which is expected to add a new year to the first cycle of secondary
education in 2007-08 and to reach the last year of the second cycle in 2010-11.
b. Pre-service training. As for primary education, this activity seeks to define new secondary teacher
profiles in terms of required pedagogical skills, linguistic skills, professional conduct and
subject-knowledge competences, and to reorient pre-service training accordingly.
(Phase 1) This component will support the development of competency and skill profile/referential for
teachers and inspectors in secondary schools. The profile/referential will cover essential skills such as,
lesson planning & preparation, classroom management, student assessment, communication and facilitation
skills to engage community in school activities, etc. Similarly, training of teachers and inspectors will be
designed based on the profile/referential, which will be essential for better recruitment, supervision, and
support for teachers. Practical training component will be lengthened and strengthened through integration
of the "professionalization" module.
(Phase 2) The training programs will be consolidated.
c. In-service training. In-service training will be used mainly to prepare teachers to perform in a
bilingual environment and to provide professional development opportunities.
(Phase 1) During the first phase, research will be conducted to identify cost-effective ways to train
"potentially bilingual" teachers, such as intensive French-language training, language immersion, distance
training packages, etc. Training needs assessments will also be conducted to develop in-service
professional development programs, in accordance with the new profiles.
(Phase 2) The training process and outcome will be evaluated, and successful models will be expanded for
nation-wide implementation in the second phase.
d. Development and distribution of textbooks and equipment. The sub-component will support the
development and distribution of good-quality textbooks and teachers' guides for key subjects, laboratory
and computer equipment, and libraries to secondary schools. Secondary schools will also be eligible for
grants to finance school improvement projects.
(Phase 1) In accordance with the new curriculum, the textbook and teachers' guides will be developed,
tested, validated, and then disseminated. To promote and reinforce science education at secondary level,
science labs will be provided to colleges and lycees.
(Phase 2) Textbooks and teachers' guides developed during the phase 1 will be assessed and revised
accordingly. The investments in equipment for laboratories and libraries will be completed during Phase 2.
The grants for school improvement programs will be taken to scale.
e. Monitoring and Supervision. Monitoring and supervision activities will be shifted from a "policing"
tool to "facilitation" and "support" tool for teachers.
- 50 -



(Phase 1) New standardized inspection practices will be developed. Inspectors will be retrained and better
equipped logistically to carry out more frequent and meaningful supervision activities.
(Phase 2) The new curriculum and its implementation for the 2nd cycle of secondary education will be
evaluated.
Subcomponent 1-3. Promoting ECD Activities to enhance Physical. socio-emotional. and cognitive
development of children
Key strategies and Activities
This subcomponent aims to promote an integrated approach to the development of 3 to 6-year-old children
(physical, socio-emotional, and cognitive) by providing training and promoting the creation of new ECD
centers. This sub-component especially targets community and private initiatives in selected rural areas.
a. Training of ECD personnel. The sub-component will aim to develop a system of pre- and in-service
training for personnel who are engaged in ECD activities, particularly those personnel who have not
received any formal training prior to being hired by communities.
(Phase 1) Five existing resource centers for ECD at regional level will be reinforced, and 8 new centers
will be developed to support the ECD activities operated by the communities, and 3 existing centers will be
rehabilitated. In addition to those resource centers, a new institution, the National Center of ECD will be
established to coordinate and carry out the in-service training activities of ECD personnel and to prepare
and provide pre-service training modules for trainers and teachers of the ECD centers in urban areas. The
National Center of ECD will also conduct action research on ECD. Before the end of Phase 1, these
activities will be evaluated with a view to expanding their coverage during Phase 2.
(Phase 2) The objective of Phase 2 will be to consolidate the activities of Phase 1 and expand the systems
and structures to support the ECD activities piloted in Phase 1. At the same time, the new experiments in
in-service training and research will continue to inform innovative ways of delivering ECD services.
b. Management Capacity Building for ECD. To enhance the quality and coverage of service, the
management capacity of ECD personnel will be enhanced.
(Phase 1) The institutional framework for ECD activities will be defined, and the intersectoral committee
for ECD will be formed with the participation of SECF, MEN, MSAS, UNICEF, and other key actors.
The necessary material and human resources will be supplied for a better planning of ECD activities. The
system of monitoring and evaluation as well as a basic database of ECD programs, personnel and training
activities will be developed. To promote sensitization to ECD issues, communication strategies and tools
will be developed, targeting various audiences such as community leaders, opinion leaders, parents and the
civil society. Sensitization campaign activities will be planned, their outcome will be evaluated, and
successful actions will be expanded.
(Phase 2) During the Phase 2, the system will be decentralized. Periodic monitoring and evaluation
mechanisms will be institutionalized.
c. Promoting Community and Private Initiatives for ECD. The objective of this activity is to promote
community-based ECD programs.
- 51 -



(Phase 1) Appropriate ECD activities will be developed for the community nursery and preschools. The
teaching-learning materials and first aid kit will be distributed to conmmunity nursery and preschools in the
targeted areas, within the available resources at national level.
(Phase 2) Based on the experiences of Phase 1, the activity will be expanded for a wider coverage.
Sub-Comnonent 14. Promotine School Health
Key Strategies and Activities
This component seeks to improve the health status of students by promoting school hygiene and preventive
care, and reducing the prevalence of micronutrient deficiencies. The distribution of drugs will be piloted in
a limited number of regions before being expanded during phase two.
a. Promoting good practice of hygiene and nutrition at school-level.
(Phase 1) To create awareness and promote good practice on hygiene and nutrition at school level: (i)
teachers will be trained on hygiene, nutrition and school health issues, (ii) school health and nutrition guides
will be prepared and 10,000 copies will be distributed to schools, (iii) radio and TV spots will be
broadcast.
(Phase 2) The activities during Phase 1 will be extended to all schools.
b. Promoting preventive health care
(Phase 1) A vaccination campaign will be organized at school level and, based on national surveys of
children's health and nutrition status, de-worming tablets and micronutrients will be distributed to students
to reduce the prevalence of iodine, iron and vitamin A deficiencies.
(Phase 2) Continuation of the hygiene and nutrition activities and expansion to all schools of
preventive-care activities.
Project Component 2 - US$132.30 million
Component 2. Promoting Access to and Equity in Education
Objective
The objective of the second component is to promote access to literacy and basic education, and to improve
the inter- and intra- regional and gender equity in education, especially at primary and secondary levels.
IDA will finance $15.8 million, and AfDB will finance $3.5 million for Component 2. The Govemment
contribution will be $96.1 million, HIPC $4.3 million, and local communities $4.9 million.
Key Indicators
*   59% of the primary schools offer a complete cycle (6 years of education) by 2005, and 100% by
2010.
- 52 -



*   Girls enrollnent in primaly education increases to 48% in 7 disadvantaged regions.
*   50% of the students completing primary education will access secondary education (although the
percentage remains the same throughout most of the current decade, the number of students
accessing grade 1 of secondary education will increase from the current 18,000 to 25,000 in 2005,
and to 30,000 in 2010 as more students will complete primary education.)
*   Gender parity in secondary education is reached in 2010
Subcomponent 2-1. Universal Access to Primary Education by 2005
Key Strategies and Activities
The main strategy to increase access in primary education is to transform existing incomplete schools into
complete schools with additional classrooms and multigrade teaching. At the same time, studies will be
conducted to identify the gender and regional disparities and to develop appropriate action plans.
a. Improving Planning Capacity
The objective of the component is to achieve appropriate planning of all educational inputs at the central
and regional levels and to achieve respect of the school mapping criteria by key stakeholders (Walis,
Mayors, etc.). The DPC will be responsible for this sub-component. The French Cooperation, IDA, and
AfDB will support the first phase.
(Phase 1). The objective of this phase will be to build planning capacities at the central and regional levels.
This sub-component will also reinforce the planning process and procedures and improve school mapping
tools to identify optimal steps and necessary inputs to transform the incomplete-cycle schools into complete
ones. This will be achieved through training of an adequate number of staff at the central and regional
levels, the development of participatory planning procedures including communities and regional and local
stakeholders, and the provision at each level of adequate planning tools. Data from the recently completed
census and LSMS surveys will be exploited to identify demand issues and causes of regional disparities in
access, repetition, and dropout, and specific actions will be developed.
(Phase 2) The planning responsibilities will be decentralized to the regional level while the central level
will ensure monitoring and evaluation, and support as needed, of the regionally executed planning.
b. Building Classrooms and equipping schools with latrines.
To achieve universal primary education this activity seeks to build adequate school facilities to
accommodate an increase of nearly 200,000 students (1998: 346,000; 2010: 546,000). Schools will be
retrofitted with latrines, office-storage and water supply. The DPEF will be responsible for this
sub-component, while the execution of the AfDB and IDA-financed constructions will be delegated to
eligible communities (PTA) under sub-projects contracted between PTA and DPEF, according to
procedures that have proven their effectiveness over the last 10 years. Under these arrangements,
communities are granted by the Government up to 70% of the estimated classroom cost and 100% of the
cost of other school facilities, such as latrines and Director offices. The first phase of this sub-component is
supported by IDA, through this project and the UDP, AfDB, FRD, CDHLCPI
(Phase 1) . The objectives of phase 1 are to: (i) complete incomplete schools through either the construction
of additional classrooms, the use of multigrade teaching, or a combination of both; (ii) provide access to
school to all 6-year old children at the end of the phase; (iii) replace 40% (about 800) of existing
- 53 -



classrooms built in non-permanent materials; (iv) test a new maintenance strategy based on transfer to
schools of maintenance funds. At the end of Phase 1 2,700 classroom will be built.
(Phase 2) During the second phase, construction and rehabilitation activities will continue at a rate of
about 600 classrooms per year. The program will give priority to the scaling-up of the maintenance
strategy tested in Phase 1.
c. Incentives for teachers in hardship areas. To improve equity in access, it is essential to deploy an
adequate number of qualified teachers in remote areas.
(Phase 1) Criteria for "hardship area" will be defined, and an incentive mechanism for teachers to serve in
these areas will be designed and implemented. Teachers in these areas who can potentially become
bilingual teachers will be identified through a survey, and they will receive priority for retraining.
(Phase 2) An incentive mechanism developed in Phase I will be implemented and evaluated.
Subcomponent 2-2. Increasing Access to and Equity in Secondary Education
Key strategies and activities
With the number of students completing primary school on the increase, the demand for secondary
education is inevitably on a sharp rise. To meet the demand for schooling at that level in a systematic and
financially feasible way, the transition rate from the primary to the 1 st cycle of the secondary will be
limited to 50% through 2010, although the absolute number of the students will increase.
a. Reinforcing planning capacity.
Complementary to new construction and rehabilitation, effective school rmapping will be instrumental to
realize an optimal distribution and construction of facilities. Coordination between the central Govermment
and key stakeholders such as NGOs, community, and school management committee will be strengthened.
(Phase 1) New planning procedures, involving parents and local authorities in decision-making will be
piloted. The persons responsible for school mapping for secondary education in DPC and in the regional
offices will be designated and trained. The office responsible for school mapping will be equipped with
computers. The database for school mapping will become operational.
(Phase 2) The planning procedures will be generalized during the second phase; regional antennas will be
made operational with necessary equipment and the capacity to make decisions locally. Two technical staff
will be trained in school data collection and analysis in each region.
b. Improving Access.
In order to increase access to the I st cycle of secondary education, both human and physical resources will
be expanded, and particular attention will be paid to gender issues, which are more acute at this level than
in primary education. DPC and DES will be responsible for this activity.
(Phase 1) 600 teachers (150 each year) will be recruited; 30 new colleges with 8 classrooms, 160
extension classrooms, and 40 new latrines will be built; 1,000 classrooms and 300 latrines will be
rehabilitated; 250 computers, 50,000 student desks and benches, 700 office shelves, 500 administrative
- 54-



office desks, and 1,300 desks for teachers will be supplied. In addition, equipment for one teacher's room
per collge/lycee will be supplied. The Government will also promote access of girls to secondary
education through a pilot program of parent-operated boarding facilities, supported by several UN donors.
Based on the findings of the study and the pilot program, appropriate actions and communication strategies
will be developed to promote girls' schooling.
(Phase 2) 1,000 teachers (200 each year) will be recruited; 34 new collIges, 100 classrooms, and 60
latrines (to extend existing colleges) will be built; and 500 office desks, 700 desks for teachers, and 500
shelves will be supplied.
Sub-component 2-3: Promotin2 the Private Sector Involvement in Education.
With increasing demand for education, the role of the private sector will be crucial in complementing the
public provision of education. This sub-component primarily concerns secondary, vocational, and higher
education institutions to supplement the Government effort at those levels and promote diversify and
innovation.
(Phase 1) The sub-component will explore appropriate regulations and mechanisms to support and
promote the private sector and establish institutional and legal frameworks to monitor the private sector and
implement quality assurance mechanisms. A grants fund will be implemented to benefit private sector
institutions. The fund's guidelines and operation procedures will be developed during the first phase of the
ESDP.
(Phase 2) The institutional framework and the fund to support private schools will be evaluated and
improved as necessary.
Subcomponent 24. Promoting Functional Literacy and Non-Formal Education
Key Strategies and Activities
A principal strategy for improving literacy in Mauritania is to (i) develop the capacity of the SEAEO to
plan and manage the new literacy programs; (ii) operate a better linkage between the modem education
system and the traditional Mahadras, by promoting twinning arrangements between individual schools and
Mahadras with a view to facilitating the transition of students between the formal education and the
Mahadras systems. It is expected that 24% of the 1,700 Mahadras will have been "modernized" in 2005
against currently 8%; (iii) gradually phase out the national literacy campaigns and replace them by a
variety of functional literacy programs, developed and operated by NGOs and communities. These
functional literacy programs will be adapted to local needs, targeted to vulnerable populations, and aimed
at promoting income generation.
(Phase 1) The following activities will be carried out, on a pilot basis, by participating literacy providers
and promoted and coordinated by the SEAEO: (i) development of functional literacy programs that are
relevant to identified economic and social needs and specifically targeted populations; (ii) mobilization of
various actors (NGOs, comnunity groups, professional associations) to design and deliver literacy
programs; (iii) capacity building of the core literacy personnel, training of material developers and trainers;
(iv) action research and development of teaching-learning materials and assessment of the outcome of the
activities. The implementation of the new modes for literacy support requires the development of detailed
procedures and monitoring mechanisms, through the provision of technical assistance and the staff
exchange with countries that have relevant experience.
- 55 -



(Phase 2) The objective is to take to scale the activities of Phase 1.
Subcomponent 2-5. Promotin! Girls Education
Key Strategies and Activities
The objective of this subcomponent is to continue the successful activities started under the IDA V project
and to imnprove the knowledge base relative to the determinants of the main obstacles to girls access and
retention.
(Phase 1) The public awareness campaigns are continued. Small-scale colleges and community-based
accommodations will be piloted to promote access of girls in rural areas. In order to improve the
enrollment and retention of girls, a study will be conducted to identify possible causes of girls'
non-attendance and dropout particularly in the 7 low-enrollment regions. Based on the findings of the
study, appropriate sensitization and communication strategies will be developed to promote girls' schooling
both at primary and secondary levels.
(Phase 2) The successful activities will be consolidated, particularly the measures aimed at increasing the
access of girls in rural areas.
Project Component 3- USS 64.79 million
Component 3. Improving the Efficiency of the Technical, Vocational and Higher Education Systems
Objective
The overall objective of the third component is to foster and strengthen two-way linkages between
technical, vocational and higher education and the labor-market. Shorter and more flexible training
programs will be emphasized as well as more job-related content in the curricula, consultation with
enterprises and greater autonomy and accountability of training and higher education institutions.
The contributions of various development partners on Component 3 will be the following: IDA $1 1.7
million, AfDB $11.4 million, Cooperation Francaise $0.01 million, and Islamic Development Bank $3.8
million. The Government will finance $23.4 million, HIPC $3.3 million, and the private sector will
contribute $0.3 million.
Key Indicators
* The private sector represents 20% in the service delivery of technical and professional training by
2005.
* Technical and vocational training with apprenticeship and dual program represents 200 trainees by
2005 and 500 in 2010.
*   Students in professionally oriented courses in the university represent 15% of total enrollment by
2005.
*   The internal efficiency of higher education institutions increases from 23% to 28% in 2005 and to
60% in 2010.
Subcomponent 3-1. Makin2 Technical and Professional Training More Responsive to Market
Demands
- 56 -



Key Strategies and Activities
The principal strategy is to make the technical and professional training more responsive to the demand of
the labor market. To this end, the policy consists in (i) using innovative financing mechanisms to promote
demand-driven training programs, as well as flexible and shorter training programs for the non-formal
sector, (ii) developing an adequate institutional framework to manage funds and promote the renovation of
the training programs and (iii) providing incentives to promote private TVT institutions.
a. Establishment of a Technical and Vocational Training Fund (TVTF). This Fund's objective is to
help enterprises better define and articulate the private sector demands for training (including
apprenticeship, intemship, sandwich program, etc.) and to encourage the training institutes to meet these
demands. Capitalizing upon the experience of Fonds d 'Appui aux Actions de Formation (FAAF) which
was piloted in a previous IDA project (Education IV), the Fund will finance 80%/0 of eligible in-service
training activities proposed by enterprises, or new pre-service training programs developed jointly by
enterprises and public or private training centers, based on agreed eligibility criteria. The establishment of
the Fund was already approved by the Government, and the procedure manual has been prepared and will
be finalized by the Fund's Board after its appointment. The Fund will be managed by a Board which has
an equal number of representatives from the Government and the private sector.
(Phase 1) The administrative structure and procedures for the Fund operation will be established and the
Fund will be operational. The TVTF is expected to have a significant impact by 2005, with more than 200
trainees per year in apprenticeship training schemes and 10% of the formal enterprises which have more
than 10 employees participating in in-service training activities. With the Fund's financial support, new
training modes and programs, such as internship, apprenticeship and sandwich program will be developed
by the training centers, tested, and evaluated. Because of the shorter and dual nature of the new training
programs, an increase of the total number of trainees from 2,450 to 5,100 is expected by the year 2005.
(Phase 2) The Fund's financing mechanisms and administrative procedures will be adjusted and improved
on the basis of several years of operation, in order to expand its capacity to promote innovative training
schemes. By 2010, 25% of the 'formal' sector enterprises that have more than 10 employees will be
participating in in-service training programs and a total of 2000 trainees will be enrolled in apprenticeship
training schemes. The total number of trainees will reach 10,000 by 2010.
b. Institutional Framework for Technical and Professional Training.
In order to promote the quality and effectiveness of training activities, the institutional framework of the
TVT system will be reinforced through: (i) effective coordination mechanisms among the various
ministries that manage training activities or operate training centers, (ii) permanent consultation with the
private enterprises and the private training institutions, and (iii) monitoring the labor market demands as
well as the performance of the training programs through the insertion rate of their graduates; and (iv)
managing the training fund. This institutional framework is composed of:
*   An inter-ministerial committee, chaired by the minister of education, and including all ministries
involved in TVT training. This Committee will review sector policies and coordinate training
programs.
* A National Institute for the Promotion of Technical and Professional Training (L 'Institut National de
Promotion de la Formation Technique et Professionnelle: INP-FTP), which will be created under the
responsibility of the MEN. The INP-FTP is a structure which capitalizes on the pilot Resource and
- 57 -



Monitoring Center (Centre de Ressources et de Suivi) established under the Education IV project. It
will have the following responsibilities: (i) management of TVTF, (ii) monitoring of labor market
demand and identification of training needs, (iii) provision of in-service training to upgrade the skills of
the training instructors, (iv) monitoring and evaluation of the perfornance of the training programs,
and (v) providing technical support to the training centers for the renovation of the upgrading or
renewal of their training programs. The INP-FTP has a administrative board that includes
representatives of the private sector.
*   The technical and Vocational Training Support Fund (TVTF) is created as a financially autonomous
body, assisted administratively by a special unit within the INP-FTP. The financing decisions are
made by the TVTF Board.
(Phase 1) The institutional framework described above will be implemented and all mechanisms and
procedures will be operational. Labor market information and enterprises' demand for skills will be
analyzed regularly to identify priority training areas. A system for validation and accreditation of the new
training programs will be developed. A training program for technical and administrative staff of
NP-FTP, as well as tutors, instructors and administrative staff in the training centers will be implemented
to develop the competences necessary to the functioning of the new system.
(Phase 2) The institutional structure established during the first phase will be reinforced during phase 2 to
quickly adapt to the needs of the economy. The evaluation of the extent to which a strong partnership with
enterprises has been established will be carried out and the lessons learned will be reflected in the future
activity and the consolidation of the successful initiatives.
c. Upgrading the human and physical capacities of the Technical and Professional Training Centers
Introducing the new modes of training program such as apprenticeship and sandwich program
(Subcomponent 3-1) requires the hiring of tutors, new instructors and trainers and the organization of
in-service training activities for the training centers technical and administrative staff. There is also a need
to upgrade the training centers facilities and training equipment.
(Phase 1) Complementary to the introduction of the new training program, instructors and tutors of the
technical and professional training will be trained through pre- and in-service training. Existing training
facilities and equipment will be upgraded. An economic and feasibility study will be required prior to each
extension or creation of a new training center.
(Phase 2) Based on the result of the phase 1 experiments, further expansion of the system capacity is
envisaged.
Subcomponent 3-2. Improving the Oualitv and Efficiency of Higher Education
Key Strategies and Activities
The main objective of this sub-component is to improve the quality, diversity, and responsiveness to labor
market demands of the higher education institutions. This requires (i) a better match between the number
of students and the teaching resources available, (ii) increased autonomy and accountability of the higher
education institutions, (iii) better use of public funds, and (iv) increased emphasis on professional degrees.
a. Enhancing the autonomy of the university
- 58 -



Higher education institutions lack administrative, financial and pedagogic autonomy. They are not allowed
to hire teaching staff under contract; there are regulatory restrictions that hamper their ability to raise funds
and to use them efficiently; and the validation of new academic programs are subject to lengthy
bureaucratic procedures. To deal with these issues the Government will develop a new law for higher
education that introduces significant changes in the statutes of the university, leading to greater autonomy
and at the same time more accountability of the higher learning institutions.
(Phase 1) The clear responsibility and the division of labor between the central administration for higher
education, DESup (La Direction de I 'Enseignement Superieur), the University of Nouakchott and each
higher education institution will be redefined and clarified. The nature and scope of academic, financial,
and administrative autonomy to be granted to each level, as well as the responsibility in terms of monitoring
and evaluation will be delineated. Strategies and performance targets for individual institutions will be
negotiated with the state, which provides funding, and materialized in a 3-year contract-program. The
contract-program emphasizes the state's funding and policy role as well as the institutions' responsibility for
reaching agreed academic results, resource mobilization targets, and efficient management of funds. These
principles will be the backbone of a law to be passed and implemented before the end of Phase 1. At the
same time, the university system will receive technical assistance training to build its capacity to manage
higher education institutions in accordance with the new framework.
(Phase 2) The institutional frarnework established during the first phase to enhance the autonomy of the
higher education institutes will be consolidated. The proportion of auto-financing of the individual
institutions will be increased.
b. Rationalizing the utilization of public funds
The funding of the university of Nouakchott and the individual institutions is neither related to the actual
needs of these institutions nor to their academic performance. This situation can be explained by the
inadequacy of public subsidies as well as the absence of rational funding mechanisms. To address these
issues the Government will increase funding available to the national institutions by reducing expenditures
on scholarships abroad, reinvest savings in academic inputs, and develop and implement a transparent and
rigorous fund allocation formula.
(Phase 1) The implementation of this strategy requires the provision of a variety of inputs, including
training, technical assistance and study tours for key administrative staff of the MEN, the university of
Nouakchott and the individual institutions. The provision of these inputs will be clustered in coherent
procurement packages, with clearly identified objectives, that will be awarded to foreign universities which
have demonstrated knowledge in resource allocation and management. These procurement packages
include such inputs as, provision of training, transfer of know-how and procedures, and technical assistance
during the design and implementation phase.
(Phase 2) To maintain the efficient use of public finance in the higher education, the administration of
scholarship will be transferred to an autonomous institution, Centre National des Oeuvres Universitaire,
which will be created to manage the nonacademic functions currently under the responsibility of the
university, such as the operation of student accommodations facilities and restaurants.
c. Improving the science and technology teaching and research
Less than 8% of the students are currently enrolled in science and technology streams. While an increase
of this proportion requires the enrollment of more students in the scientific streams of secondary education,
- 59 -



a number of improvements are necessary at the higher education level. The major improvements that are
sought include (i) the upgrading of the skills of the faculty through exchange programs with foreign
universities, (ii) a better linkage between research and teaching, and (iii) the use of new communication
and information technology to promote the delivery of lecture through video-conferencing, link with other
universities, including the African Virtual University, audio-visual training, sharing of research resources,
etc...
(Phase 1) To promote applied research in science and technology, a research fund will be created. Priority
for funding will be given to applied research which has potential impact on enhancing the teaching of
science subjects or the productivity of the economy. The quality of research will be strengthened through
training of the doctoral students in partnership with other universities. Exchange programs for researchers
with other institutions will be clustered and procured as a package of coherent and targeted inputs.
(Phase 2) In-service training of professors will be continued, and professors will be encouraged to pursue
higher academic qualifications. The criteria for the evaluation and accreditation of research will be
established during the second phase.
d. Professionalization and diversification of the courses
Higher education institutions will seek a partnership with the private sector and reinforce the linkage with
the labor market demands. Based on the study on the current labor market demands and the rate of
insertion from the higher education institutes, more diversified and professionally-oriented courses will be
offered, particularly 2-year diplomas.
(Phase 1) The development of new courses will be decided on a case by case basis, after consultation with
the private sector and potential employers, as well as feasibility studies. It will be built on the limited
experiences that were implemented in recent years with technical assistance from foreign universities. By
the end of Phase 1, 15 % of the students will be enrolled in professionally-oriented degrees.
(Phase 2) The experience with the professionally-oriented degrees and courses will be evaluated and
extended to all the institutions.
Project Component 4 - US$18.92 million
Component 4. Enhancing the Administrative, Financial, and Pedagogic Management Capacity
Objective
The main objective of this component is to enhance administrative, financial and technical (pedagogical)
knowledge and skills of key staff involved in the management system of the education sector, and to
facilitate the implementation process of the Govemment's deconcentration policy.
The contributions of various development partners for Component 4 will be the following: IDA$6.8
million, AfDB $2 million, Cooperation Francaise $2.5 million, and Islamic Development Bank $3.8
million. The Government will finance $2.0 million, HIPC $1.6 million, and the private sector will
contribute $0.5 million.
Key Indicators
- 60 -



*  Mission, terms of reference and administrative, personnel, and financial procedures of each
Directorate in MEN and DREN are clearly defined and applied by staff in 2005.
*  Information on school perfonnance (student achievement, teacher attendance) are readily available
and used for decision making by 2005.
*  Teachers are deployed in an efficient and transparent manner.
*  The responsibilities of the DREN are reinforced.
Subcomponent 4-1. Developinty Overall Monitoring Structure
Key Strategies and Activities
The objective is to implement a result-oriented monitoring and management system, by providing managers
with adequate information, standardized procedures and criteria for resource allocation.
a. Educational Management Information System
(Phase 1) The objective of this sub-component is to develop an Educational Management Information
Systems (EMIS,) which will be user-friendly, and emphasizes data analysis and action, and to make it
available for decision-making at the central level. The EMIS includes information on student, teachers,
school, exams and finance, and criteria and guideline for decision-making. The procedure for data
collection and presentation will be standardized. Key personnel at both the national and regional levels will
be trained in the use of EMIS.
(Phase 2) The EMIS will be implemented and operational both at the central and regional levels, enabling
the sharing of infornation among all the Directorates and Regional Offices.
b. Planning and School Mapping Procedures
(Phase 1) This activity will modemize the planning procedures by (i) integrating demographic and
contextual information in the decision making process, (ii) including consultation with the beneficiaries in
the decision-making process for school construction and extension, and (iii) integrate the use of modem
planning tools, such as geographic information systems, in the planning process.
(Phase 2) Deconcentration of school construction planning at regional level.
Subcomponent 4-2. Improvine the Management of Human Resources in the Education Sector
Key Strategies and Activities
The overall strategy of this sub-component is to modernize the human resource management system and
practices in order to create an institutional framework and enviromnent conducive to good performance of
teachers, inspectors, school directors, administrative staff, and MEN officers.
(Phase 1) The activities aim to: (i) reorganize the structure responsible for the human resource
management, define its mission and responsibilities, upgrade the managerial skills of it personnel; (ii)
consolidate personnel data to include administrative information and job profile in a single comprehensive
and reliable database that will become the unique source of informnation for decision-making on teaching
and non- teaching staff issues; (iii) expand the teachers career development system to include performance
incentives; (iv) contribute to rationalizing teacher deployment by developing and testing criteria for
-61 -



hardship compensation and rotation periods, as well as a monitoring system with quantitative goals, and (v)
improve staff professional behavior by developing guidelines and implementing sensitization activities for
teachers, directors and inspectors.
(Phase 2) During the second phase, necessary revisions in the system and criteria will be made, and
appropriate responsibilities in human resource management will be delegated to the regional offices.
Subcomponent 4-3. Developing a Pedagogic Monitoring System Based on Results
Key Strategies and Activities
The objective of this activity is to reduce the observed variance in the results of individual schools, by
setting up appropriate monitoring systems for school perfornance, and mechanisms to address the causes
of low performance. This includes: (i) establishing performance-based management system; (ii) setting up
a fund to support innovative school program; (iii) development of school management guidelines; and (iv)
improving exam management systems.
(Phase 1) the school perfornance assessment systems will be established and assessment results made
available to the schools to allow them to reflect on their performance. Small grants for school improvement
will be allocated to low-performing schools, based on the preparation of a school improvement plan and
eligibility criteria to be defined. For inspectors, the evaluation criteria of the schools and teachers'
performance, observation tools will be developed and standardized. The guidelines for the management of
textbooks, library books and didactic equipment at school level will be developed, printed and disseminated.
To improve equity, criteria for graduation exams at the end of primary education will gradually be
standardized, and the transition exams from primary to secondary education will be revised. Similarly, the
exam at the secondary level will be reviewed and rationalized to improve equity. An exam office will be
created to manage the technical and logistical aspects of the examination system.
(Phase 2) The feedback from phase 1 will be incorporated to improve the assessment systems and support
for inspectors. The guidelines will be modified as necessary. Based on the results from Phase 1, selection
exam and transition procedure will be consolidated.
Subcomwonent 4-4. Improving Financial and Administrative Management
Key Strategies and Activities
The objective of this component is to deconcentrate MEN responsibilities, based on the recommendations
of the management audit report completed in 2000.
(Phase 1) The following activities will be implemented: (i) promulgation of the new organization chart of
the MEN, including the establishment of the DRENs; (ii) appointment or retraining of key administrative
staff in accordance with detailed job profiles to be established, and reinforcement of the DAFM; (iii)
creation of physical structures for the DREN and allocation of human resources and equipment; and (iv)
deconcentrating relevant responsibilities to the DREN and assessing results.
(phase 2) Transfer of the implementation of the Donors-financed project from the DPEF to the MEN and
deepening of the deconcentration process.

Annex 4: Cost Effectiveness Analysis Summary
MAURITANIA: Education Sector Development Program
This Annex presents the economic analysis of the Mauritania's Education Sector Development Program
(ESDP). Specifically, the following aspects are analyzed: (1) macro economic framework, (2) the ESDP's
linkages with the PRSP and the CAS, (3) key findings from the Economic and Sector Work, (4) financing
of the education sector, (5) financial and sensitivity analysis, (6) the role of the public sector, and (7) cost
effectiveness analysis.
The economic analysis concludes that the objectives and strategies of the ESDP are congruent and mutually
supportive with the PRSP 2000 and the CAS 1997 and viable in the current and prospective
macroeconomic framework. The achievement of good quality basic education for all with higher
completion and achievement rates would be instrumental in developing the productivity of the poor, while
making the higher education and technical training more flexible and responsive to market demands would
contribute to the generation of human capital for the modem economic sector, thereby fueling economic
growth.
1. Macroecononmc Framework
Significant progress has been made in macroeconomic indicators as a result of the structural adjustment
policies implemented since 1992 by the Government of Mauritania. Between 1992 and 1999, the current
account deficit (excluding public transfers) fell from SDR -139.2 million to SDR -26.8 million. The
inflation rate was brought down to 4%. During this period, a policy aimed at public finance restructuring
was pursued, which led to budgetary surpluses amounting to UM 5 billion on average over the past four
years. This is a significant achievement compared to 1994 when the deficit stood at more than UM 12.5
million. The PRSP policy objective is to maintain a GDP annual growth rate of 6% on average over the
next five years.
Despite these favorable trends, Mauritania remains vulnerable from a foreign trade standpoint because of
the country's dependence on the export of primary products (iron and fish), which are subject to price and
quantity fluctuations. The vulnerability of the economy together with pressure on foreign exchange have
forced the Government to follow restrictive monetary and budgetary policies. The restructuring of public
finance and foreign debt relief from HIPC initiatives are now expected to allow controlled relief of
monetary and budgetary policies.
2. Linkages with the CAS and Poverty Reduction Strategy
The Education Sector Development Program is closely linked with, and mutually supportive of the key
themes of the PRSP. The PRSP 2000 is built upon four main themes: (i) accelerating economic growth
through its direct and indirect impact on job creation and through its impact on the Govemment's budget
revenues, (ii) developing the growth potential and productivity of the poor, (iii) developing human resources
and access to essential infrastructure, and (iv) promoting true institutional development based upon good
govemance and on full participation of all parties involved in poverty alleviation.
The Mauritanian economy is, and will likely remain, a dual economy in the foreseeable future, combining a
modem sector and a traditional economy. The challenge for the education sector is to meet the demand for
this dual economy. Good quality basic education for all, which is one of the major objectives of the ESDP,
- 64 -



would directly contribute to increase the productivity of the poor and develop the human capital basis.
Technical and vocational training and higher education which are more flexible and geared to the market
demands would contribute to meeting the demand of the modem economic sector in the country.
Strengthening the management system and practices in the education sector would be a critical part of the
fourth theme of the PRSP, institutional development and good governance.
Specifically, the PRSP 2000 states that "the Government's education strategy aims to ensure for each
Mauritanian child ten years of basic schooling (6 years of primary plus 4 years of the first cycle of
secondary school), adapted to the cultural milieu and accessible to all, followed by training opportunities
tailored to current and future labor market demands." The envisaged outcomes of the ESDP support these
goals. An effective implementation of the ESDP would contribute to poverty alleviation through its effects
on overall economic growth (productivity gains through improved quality and relevance of education and
training contents and other externalities such as improved women's health) and improved equity in
education (empowerment of the poor and disadvantaged groups through universal access to basic
education).
In the rural areas where 76% of the poor live, low productivity is an important factor for poverty. The
analysis from the household survey shows a correlation between years of schooling and the poverty level.
The proposed program would help rural children have access and complete basic education with better and
more relevant knowledge and skills for further education and/or for employment. To address equity and
target the disadvantaged, the first phase of the proposed APL envisages several studies, including a study to
identify the cause of regional and gender disparity in retention and student achievement, and a study to
identify the causes of girls dropout. The Government's ten-year education sector program is clearly
pro-poor with its focus on access, retention, and quality of basic education in rural and semi-urban areas
where the poor are concentrated.
3. Key Issues Identified in the Education Sector Study 2000
In developing the policy objectives of the Education Sector Development Program, the education sector
analysis (Le systeme educatif mauritanien: elements d 'analyse pour instruire des politiques nouvelles,
May 2000) played a critical role in identifying key issues, defining sector priorities, and developing
strategies. The sector study was prepared in the context of HIPC initiative. The sector-study team consists
of key staff from the MAED, MEN, Ministry of Finance, and the University of Nouakchott, with technical
assistance from the World Bank. Some of the key issues in the sector study which are relevant to the
ESDP are presented below.
Access
Access to primary education expanded rapidly during the 1990s; the gross enrollment rate in primary
education increased from 41.5% in 1985/86 to 85.5% in 1998/99. Gross enrollment rates at the first cycle
of secondary school (grade 7-9) and at higher education marked a modest increase from 14.8% in 1985/86
to 20.4% in 1998/99, and from 3.8% in 1985/86 to 6.8% in 1998/99 respectively. The GERs in the second
cycle of secondary education and vocational and technical training remained approximately at the same
level since 1985.

Iternal Efficiency
Although considerable progress has been made to access, internal efficiency remains low throughout the
education system. First, repetition rates at each grade level are high, and they have improved only slightly
in primary and first cycle of secondary education during the last 15 years, as shown in the table below.
The repetition rate in second cycle of secondary education has improved in recent years, but remains high
in the last grade ("terminale") as students repeatedly fail the baccalaureate exam.

Second, the retention rate also remains low, especially at the primary level. The retention rate of students
from Grade I to Grade 6 was 55% in 1996-99. The retention rate of the 1st cycle secondary school (lAS
to 3AS) has decreased from 84% in 1991-94 to 71% in 1996-99. Although the retention rate at the 2nd
cycle of secondary school seems better than that of primary and 1st cycle secondary, only 43% of those
who enter the 2nd cycle secondary education pass the baccalaureate exam. Based on these ratios, of 100
children of school-going age, only 7.7 receive their BAC degree at the end of secondary education. The
internal efficiency of higher education is likewise low. Only 12% of Humanities (FLSH: Faculte des
Lettres et Sciences Humaines) and Social Science (FSJE: Faculte des Sciences Juridiques et
Economiques) students obtain a graduate degree after four years.

One of the major causes of high repetition and dropout (i.e., low intemal efficiency in primary education)
in Mauritania is the existence of "incomplete" schools. As the table below shows, 23% of primary schools
offer only one grade while 18% of the schools offer 6 grades. It is often the case that schools do not offer
opportunities for students to proceed to the next grade level each year, which inevitably leads to students
repeating or dropping out from the school. The problem seems to be largely on the supply side.

Total                  2,459 (100)     330,199 (100)
Source: DPC
"Complete-cycle schools" do not necessarily mean schools with a minimum of 6 classrooms with 6 teachers
for Grade 1-6 every year. In the Mauritanian context, even if the schools offer three grade levels with each
cohort starting every other year (alternate year intake), as long as the students can proceed from one grade
to the next till Grade 6, they are considered as complete-cycle schools. Likewise, schools with multigrade
teaching in which students can advance by one grade level every year, are considered complete-cycle
schools. According to the Sector Study, if all students had access to complete-cycle schools, the current
level of retention rate would improve from the current 55% from Grade 1 to 6, to approximately 85%.
- 67 -



This is indeed the case in the schools offering continued progression from Grade I to 6, even in the rural
areas.
hnternal inefficiencies of the education system are also seen in the uneven deployment of teachers
throughout the country. The average number of the teachers per 150 students varies to a great extent: the
highest of 5.08 in Tiris Zemmour, the lowest of 1.83 in Nouadhibou, and the national average of 3.14. The
table below presents the wide variation of the student-teacher ratios (for the schools with 150 students) in
different regions of the country. In addition, the ratio varies within the region.

External Efficiency
External efficiency (feeding students into the economy) of the system is low because there is little
collaboration between the technical and vocational training systems, higher education streams, and the
labor market in terms of training programs, development and implementation, financing of the training,
and feedback between employers, employees and training institutions. The small scale of the formal
modem economic sector which is still in the early development stage - and dominance of the informal
sector -- also constrains external efficiency of the education system.
The lack of an established national policy in the past has led to the growth of a student population poorly
equipped for available employment opportunities. A mechanism to regulate student flows has not been
established, resulting, therefore, in a surplus of students, as compared to countries with socio-economic
indicators similar to those of Mauritania. Furthermore, a lack of information regarding the country's
quantitative and qualitative needs for human resources with higher education has led to a fairly large
number of unemployed graduates. As the table below shows, the numbers of unemployed degree-holders in
the Humanities (Faculte des Lettres et Sciences Humaines: FLSH) and in Social Science (Faculte des
Sciences Juridiques et Economiques: FSJE) are increasing; 41.2% in FLSH and 35.1% in FSJE in 1998.

Methodological training techniques have not changed with the times in Mauritania. There is still a
noticeable emphasis on theoretical lectures and little applied training. In addition, the proportion of
students majoring in Science and Technology at the University of Nouakchott has traditionally been and
remains surprisingly low at less than 10%.

Ouality
Quality improvement in education is one of the most critical needs of Mauritania's Education system, and
various factors appear to be responsible for its current poor quality: the lack of pedagogical support,
inefficient human resource management system-wide, insufficient textbooks and teaching materials both for
students and teachers (less than one-third of students in primary school had a textbook and one-fourth of
teachers had a textbook or guide in 1998), and high student-teacher ratios, particularly in the urban area.
Students at the primary level on average master 50% of the intended curriculum content, while students in
the worst-perfonning schools acquire only 10% of the intended curriculum against 80% in the best
performing schools. The success rate for BEPC is 61% for males and 55% for females.
- 69 -



Equit(
Although Mauritania does better than neighboring Western African countries in regards to equity in
education, gender- and regional- equity can and must be improved. At the end of the primary cycle (Grade
6), 63.8% of girls in urban areas have access to ongoing education, while only 38.4% of girls in rural areas
have access to local schools. The secondary education enrollment rate for males exceeds that of females by
about 10%.
4. Education Finance
Public resources for the education sector has increased steadily over the last 15 years. Intra-sectoral
distribution of the recurrent budget has also changed. The percentage of recurrent budget expenditure in
primary education against the total expenditure has increased steadily from 32.3% in 1985 to 43.3% in
1998 while that of higher education has decreased from 28.4% in 1985 to 18.6% in 1998. The share of
TVT has also decreased, from 6% in 1985 to 4% in 1998.

5. Financial and Sensitivity analysis
The Government of Mauritania has prepared its 10-year education sector development program based on
the simulation of key sets of policies and the macro-economic framework. Available public finance and
recurrent cost needs are calculated based on the data on key indicators in 1998, a projection of these
indicators and the policy objectives for 2010.
Following is a summary of the main results of the simulation and sensitivity analysis. Detailed financial
analysis is in Annex 11. The base scenario was built on the following projections and Government policies.
The annual growth rate of GDP would remain at 6% through 2010 as envisaged in the PRSP. The
percentage of the recurrent cost for education in the total national revenue would rise from the 13.5% in
1998 to 16.6% in 2010 as indicated in the Government sector policy. Access to Grade 1 in primary
education would rise from 90% in 1998 to 100% by 2010. Retention rate in primary education would rise
from 55% in 1998 to 78% by 2010 with all primary schools offering a complete cycle. According to the
Government policy, the teachers salary for primary education would decline to 4.7 times per capita GDP in
2010 from the 1998 level of 5.1. In the projection model, the teacher salary is linked with the GDP. The
Government intends to reduce the student-teacher ratio in primary education from 48:1 in 1998 to 40:1 by
2010. It also plans to increase the proportion of non-salary recurrent expenditure as a percentage of total
teacher salary from 17.5% in 1998 to 30% in 2010. In the first cycle of secondary education, the
Government intends to maintain the transition rate from the primary education Grade 6 to Grade I of
secondary education at the current level of 50%. It is envisaged that the percentage of students in private
- 71 -



secondary schools would increase from 7% in 1998 to 15% by 2010. The number of students in the VTE
is expected to increase from 1,682 in 1998 to 6,600 by 2010. The Government plans to control the
enrollment in public university from 9,805 in 1998 to 7,500 in 2010.

The balance estimated in the proposed ESDP (base scenario) is negligible, approximately 140 million UM
(less than 1% of the revenue) in 2010. In the second scenario with the GDP annual growth rate of 5%, the
balance between the revenue and recurrent cost would increase to UM -497 million, 3% of the revenue. In
the third scenario, if the annual GDP growth rate remains 4%, the balance in 2010 is estimated at UM -819
million, or 6% of the revenue. The fiscal sensitivity of GDP growth rate is relatively low as the teachers
salary is linked to per-capita GDP. One percent decrease in the GDP growth rate would lead to an increase
of approximately 330 million UM (2% of the revenue) of the financial gap. However, even if the country
maintains a GDP annual growth rate of 6%, if the public finance for education is kept at 13% through
- 73 -



2010, the balance would increase to -3817 million UM (28% of the revenue) as shown in scenario 5.
Therefore, the financial balance is much more sensitive to public resource allocation to education than to
the annual GDP growth rate. However, given the Government's conmnitment to the education sector for the
next ten years, the risk would not be a major one.
Sensitivity of some of the key policy decision indicators were also analyzed: primary teacher salary,
transition rate from primary to secondary, transition rate from the 3rd to newly created 4th year of the
secondary education, and the number of intake in lyc&es.

As shown in Scenario 6, if the ratio of teacher salary to per capita GDP is kept at the current level of 5.1
through 2010, the balance between the revenue and the recurrent cost would be UMM -891 million (6% of
the revenue). Ten percentage points increase in the transition rate from primary to secondary education
would lead to a balance of -860 million UM (5% of the revenue). The change in the transition rate of 3rd
year to 4th year of the first cycle of secondary school would lead to a less significant financial gap, an
increase from 50% to 70% would result in an increase of $167 million UM (less than 1% of the revenue).
Lastly, if the intake of the lyc6e increases from 9,000 in 1998 to 10,000 by 2010 (the policy is to reduce to
6,500 by 2010), there would be a substantial financial gap of 891 million UM in the recurrent cost.
6. Role of the Public Sector
Public intervention in the ESDP is justified on many grounds. First, investment in the education sector has
externalities on health, population, participation, governance, etc. (there is enough global research to show
the social return of education). Second, from the equity perspective, the state has the obligation to ensure
that every child has access to good quality basic education. Third, there is an agreement in the HIPC
initiative to invest more in education as the public finance restructuring is coming to an end.
Nevertheless, the nature and the degree of public intervention in the education sector in Mauritania need to
change in the long-term. Currently, the Government is the "provider" of the service with weak management
capacity, but eventually it would be desirable to shift its role to quality monitoring and facilitation of the
services to be provided. At present, the role of the private sector in the provision of educational services is
very limited, but this should change as the demand for education increases and diversifies. The ESDP has
the policy objective to increase the participation of the private sector in the design and the delivery of
educational services, especially at secondary, TVT, and tertiary levels. Public-private and school-business
partnerships in education will be encouraged through the development of an institutional framework and
incentive mechanisms for private participation. These mechanisms will include funds to promote
-74 -



intemships and professionalized programs in technical and vocational training, and university partnership
programs with the private sector.

TVT                   0                    0           0          0          0         TBD
Higher                0          0         0           0          0          0         TBD
Source: Le systeme educatif mauritanien elements d'analyse pour instruire despolitiques nouvelles. 2000
7. Cost-effectiveness analysis
The sector study (Le systeme educatifmauritanien: elements d 'analyse pour instruire des politiques
nouvelles, May 2000) carried out a number of cost-effectiveness analyses to better understand the relations
between school pedagogic and organizational structure, availability of educational inputs, and the
performance of schools. The study was conducted on a sample 429 schools for which adequate
information existed, from a total number of 2,656 schools. This sample had some methodological
deficiencies: the average school size in the sample was larger than the national average and a few regions
were over-represented. However, the sample included a large variety of school types in terms of
endowment, pedagogic styles, organizational structures, and academic performance, enabling the analysis
of the relation between those factors. School performance was measured by results in the primary
education completion exam (CEF). The average pass rate in the CEF was 37% for all schools in the
sample, with a very high standard deviation (26%).
Effect of the number of students per division/per teacher and the types of class arrangement on the
success of primary education certificate
Coefficient  Significance Coefficient  Significance  Coefficient  Significance
Constant                  0.49                   0.50                    0,38
# of student/division    -0.003
# of student/teacher                            -0.002
Single-grade teaching                                                     Ref
multigrade-grade teaching                                                -0.009       ns
Double shift                                                             -0.08        **
% variance explained-     4.6         5.7         1.1   I
Source: Le systeme educatif mauritanien elements d 'analyse pour instruire des politiques nouvelles. 2000
Analyzing the effect of class size, student-teacher ratio, single/multigrade teaching and double shifts, it
appears that double shifting has a significant negative impact on the success rate of the Primary Education
Certificate exam, while the size and the significance of the multigrade teaching coefficient seems to indicate
that this type of school organization has no noticeable impact on school performance. On the other hand,
both the student per teacher ratio and the class size are associated with lower school performance. This
- 75 -



result was anticipated given that the average class size was 47.4 (with a standard deviation of 21) and the
average student per teacher ration was 51.8 (standard deviation 25,) which are both relatively high. A
difference of 10 students more per class is associated with a 3% decrease in the results in the CEF.
Another noticeable result was the apparent weak impact of teachers formal qualification. The teacher body
is very diverse. About 90% of the teachers are fully qualified, 9% are partially qualified (instituteurs
adjoints) and 1% non qualified. However, in some schools, as many as 20% of the teachers are either
partially qualified or unqualified. The percentage of the variance explained suggests that the composition
of the teacher body did not have a large effect on school performance. However, paradoxically the
existence of partially qualified teachers and French-speaking teachers appear to have a positive and
significant effect.
