Additional
Annex 11
Sectoral Policy Letter
MAURITANIA: Education Sector Development Program
(Unofficial Translation)
Introduction
The 10-year assessment of the sector shows that substantial progress has been made especially with regard
to the quantitative development at the primary level of education, and to a lesser extent, at the secondary
level. In fact, the percentage of children gaining access to lower secondary falls below expectations. Only
55% complete the primary level.
Upper secondary education suffers from overcrowding due to the absence of a policy regulating the flow of
students through the system. The quality of teaching is irregular and there are disparities between the two
levels of teaching. The management of the system is inadequate. In addition, higher education, as well as
technical and vocational training are not relevant to the requirements of the development of the national
economy.
Law 99-012 on educational reform was adopted on April 26, 1999. This reform is aimed at consolidating
the accomplishments of the past while making the system definitely more open. The main pillar of the
reform entails the following:
*   Unifying the education system by establishing a single stream;
*   Strengthening first cycle of secondary education by extending it by one year (total of 4 years) and
teaching of the sciences by introducing physics and computer science;
*   Strengthening the teaching of foreign languages (French, English).
Within the framework of this reformn and with a view to correcting the inadequacies observed, the
Government has decided to establish a ten-year development program in the education sector. In this
context, our country's eligibility under the HIPC debt reduction initiative provides an opportunity for our
education system to benefit from additional resources which will enable it to make significant progress both
quantitatively and qualitatively. This prospect is consistent with the will of the Government to engage in a
process of sustained economic and social development.
I- Key issues to be addressed for the Development of the Education System in Mauritania
The ten-year education policy is based on the following objectives:
*   Strengthening the management system based on planning capacity, reorganizing the central
administration, on-going evaluation of the system and planning of recruitrnent;
*   Increasing education supply; this will necessitate, for example, creating a school location map, as well
as reorganizing university and technical and vocational training;
*   Enhancing the quality of teaching through the renovation of programs, educational material, fostering
professionalism and reorganizing initial and in-service training as well as strengthening administrative
- 107 -



and educational management;
* Improving human resource management by ensuring a performance-based merit system for career
planning and rationalization of postings;
*   Removing regional disparities under a voluntary policy for allocating resources;
* Increased schooling for girls-a key pillar of Government policy;
*   Increasing the role of the private sector in education through the implementation of an appropriate
policy.
II- Objectives of the Educational Policy According to Education Level
11-1 Basic Education
The main objectives of the primary education for the 2001-2010 period are as follows:
(1)    Improved access and student retention
Achieving universal access in the medium term (2005) making it possible to admit about 85,000 new
students in IAF as opposed to the current 66,275, will bring about a significant improvement in the
retention rate at this level (78% in 2010 against 55% currently) and entail the following measures:
* Widespread implementation of schools offering all levels by 2010. These structural changes will entail
use of the multigrade system in schools in rural areas;
- Rationalizing management and teacher posting system;
* Reducing regional disparities;
* Constructing and equipping about 530 additional classrooms per year;
* increasing teaching staff in the public schools estimated at 6,400 for the period;
* Admit roughly 30,000 children in the private sector by 2010.
(2)    Improved quality of education
This will entail:
* initial and in-service professional training for teachers and principals;
* Providing incentives for teachers appointed in disadvantaged areas;
* Gradually reducing the current student/teacher ratio of 48 to 40 by the year 2010;
*   Implementing new programs;
- 108-



*   Ensuring the availability of educational material (textbooks, guides, learning kits, etc ...);
*   Implementing a textbook policy aimed at ensuring the availability of textbooks in schools and
facilitating the liberalization of textbook publishing and distribution by 2005. To this end: (i) textbooks
for key subjects in primary and secondary education will be distributed free of charge, and (ii) other
textbooks will be sold to pupils wishing to acquire them, through the current distribution system which
will be improved (proceeds from sales will be deposited in a treasury account earmarked for the
procurement of textbooks).
*   Improving pedagogical and administrative management by making the training of middle level
managers more professional;
* Developing classroom learning assessment tools.
(3)    Implementation of the Reform:
Gradual implementation of the Reform up to the year 2004 must entail:
*   Adopting a teacher recruitment policy to meet the needs of the Reform;
*   Implementing intensive training programs for teachers who have the potential of becoming bilingual;
*   Prioritizing the posting of bilingual teachers in multigrade classes and monolingual teachers in areas
with a large concentration of students where they can carry a full teaching load.
11-2 Lower secondary education
(1) Improving the quality of education
Reform at the secondary level will entail the following:
*   Irproving the quality of pedagogical and administrative management (professional training of
inspectors and principals);
* Reducing the student/teacher ratio from 36 to 31 by the end of the period;
*   Adapting teaching programs by introducing subjects that will better prepare students for an
economically productive life;
*   Improving the production and dissemination of educational material;
*   Improved initial and in-service training of teaching personnel;
*   Developing classroom learning assessment tools.
(2)    Preparation and gradual implementation of the Reform
The Reform will affect secondary education at the beginning of the 2004 school year and its
implementation will extend to the beginning of the 2007 school year for lower secondary education. This
- 109-



will entail:
*   Progranming human resource management (recruitment and training based on new linguistic
requirements);
* Strengthening second language teaching for student cohorts entering the system prior to the Reforrn;
*   Revamping teaching programs taking into account curriculum changes in primary education, the
lengthening of the first cycle, the reorientation of its objectives and linguistic changes;
* Programming production and dissemnination of educational material.
The gradual introduction of a basic education comprising primary education and lower secondary education
will involve the reorganization of the latter. The fourth year of lower secondary education, to be introduced
in 2007 as part of the Reform, must be a year of preparation and orientation, which cannot be repeated.
(3) Reorganization of lower secondary education:
The gradual introduction of basic education will entail the extension of lower secondary education:
* Increased access to lower secondary education which will involve an estimated 35,000 new entrants by
the end of the period;
* Introduction of a certification examination;
e Measures for rethinking the crossover between basic education and the new objectives of lower
secondary education
11-3 Upper Secondary Education
The general objective of the education policy with regard to general upper secondary education is to
facilitate both qualitatively and quantitatively access to higher education. In this context, it is deemed that
effective regulation of the flow of students eligible for higher education can be accomplished by regulating
access to upper secondary education and implementing measures for independent candidates at the
end-of-cycle examination (32% of candidates in 1999). The following actions are envisaged within the
education policy:
(1)   Improving the quality of teaching.
Reform at the secondary level will entail:
* Gradual reduction of student intake, from 8,000 to 5, 000 by the end of the period;
*   Adoption of specific regulations for independent candidates at the end of second cycle examination;
* Reducing the student/teacher ratio from 23 to 16 by 2010;
*   Improving the quality of pedagogical and administrative management (professional training of
inspectors and principals);
- 110-



* Improving the production and dissemination of educational material;
* improving the initial and in-service training of teaching personnel;
* developing classroom leaming assessment tools;
* Revamping streams curricula so as to direct at least 50% of new admissions to scientific streams.
(2) Preparation and gradual implementation of the Reform.
The Reform will affect upper secondary education at the beginning of the 2008 school year and its
implementation will extend to the beginning of the 2011. This long-term undertaking will entail:
* programming human resource management (recruitment and training based on new linguistic
requirements);
e reinforcing second language teaching for groups entering the system prior to the Reform;
* Program revision based on curriculum changes, the extension of lower secondary education and
linguistic changes;
3   Programming production and dissemination of educational material.
-I-4 Technical and vocational training
The objectives defined for technical and vocational training (TVT) are aimed at improving the management
of the system, developing provision of technical education as well as specific short-term training to enable
the TVT to improve links with the other levels of education and the formal and informal sectors of
education.
(1)    Improving management of technical and vocational training
This will entail several measures:
* The establishment of the National Institute for Technical and Vocational Training (INFTP) which will
be responsible for providing technical support for decision-making and promoting technical and
vocational training. This institution will fall under the responsibility of the MEN;
* restructuring management mechanisms through a participatory process. The Director of INFTP will be
responsible for coordination and will chair the steering committee with representatives of all technical
and vocational training units in the ministries;
* establishment of a promotion fund under the responsibility of the INFTP; the fund will be financed
through contributions from businesses (learning taxes), Government and donor agencies and its
management will be shared (Government and the private sector).
- 111 -



* Implementation of demand-driven training through permanent school-to-job schemes.
(2)   Improved quality of training
This will require:
*   consolidation of in-service training for teachers as specified in the training plan;
* Renewal of educational equipment and material;
* Promoting training arrangements that combine school-based training with work experience.
(3)    Development and diversification of educational provision
The capacity of the TVT will be developed:
* quantitatively, by expanding existing institutions and creating new ones in the regions;
* qualitatively, by diversifying specialties in priority sectors;
Il-S Higher Education
The objectives of the ten-year reform program for higher education are two-fold: to improve intemal and
extemal efficiency in order to provide the country with high-caliber national managerial staff required for
its economic and social development, and to develop scientific research. This policy is built primarily
around the following points:
(1)   Improving higher education performance:
*   establishing an appropriate institutional framework (central orientation, design and coordination
structure, increased autonomy and scope for higher education institutions). In particular, a national
center will be created to improve the quality of extra-curricular services to the students. Similarly, the
management of the university restaurant and boarding facilities will be sub-contracted to the private
sector through a competitive procedure and based on clear terms of reference developed.
* Improving efficiency in public spending (gradual reduction of quotas reserved for foreign scholarships
and their reallocation to new training programs to be set up by national institutions by 2005).
(2)    Better matching of training and employment:
* controlling enTolments to reach 7,500 in 2005;
* diversifying of and fostering professionalism in training programs;
* restructuring existing training streams;
* establishing a mechanism for employment market monitoring and prospecting, and the integration of
graduates;
- 112 -



(3)   Improving the quality of higher education:
*  in-service teacher training;
*   strengthening the links between teaching and research;
*   improving pedagogical support systems (laboratories, libraries, electronic networks, NTIC, etc.);
*   Promoting the teaching function.
(4)    Gradually implementing the Reform measures:
*  promoting student teacher recruitment for the purposes of the Reform;
*  promoting the National Languages Department;
*  Introducing language teaching at the university, allowing monolingual students to receive one year of
language training.
(5)    Promoting scientific research:
*  developing research units and providing them with the appropriate tools;
*  promoting applied research;
*  encouraging the creation of research and exchange networks among researchers;
*  Establishing doctoral studies through partnership programs.
II-6 Management of the Educational System
The complexity of implementing the Reform and managing the educational system according to national
development interests demands that the practice of strategic management be developed rapidly. At the start
this involves a detailed analysis of all the problems arising from the Reform, followed by the introduction
and regular usage of modem evaluation and management techniques and tools adapted to the national
context.
1. From a sector standpoint, and as regards the Reform and its related ten-year plan, this entails: i)
defining exactly human and material requirements for Reform implementation, ii) drawing up a
schedule of actions to be taken, and iii) setting up an evaluation and monitoring mechanism to
gauge progress achieved and results obtained, and identifying the necessary adjustments in due
course.
To do this, it will be indispensable to design and build upon a set of instruments, such as: i) a
comprehensive information system using the most recent computer techniques (data bases,
simulation models, school maps, etc.), ii) specific and quantified operating plans, iii) standardized
procedures manuals, iv) a set of modernized and coherent regulations, and v) establishment and/or
strengthening of the necessary structures.
- 113-



With respect to the measures to be taken to meet the needs created by the growth in enrolments and
to ensure better management of student flows, the Ministry of National Education (MEN) will
quickly introduce an operational school map as well as reliable student files to adequately manage
the matching of educational programs with demand.
The MEN will also upgrade its examination management procedures at the primary and secondary
levels, and tighten its controls in order to reduce regional and gender disparities.
2. Apart from these sector aspects, particular emphasis will be placed on substantially improving
the system's management:
2.1 Human Resources
It has been observed that, in the current situation, the assignment of staff among the
institutions, especially at the primary level, is characterized by a high degree of uncertainty. It
is important to take the necessary measures to ensure greater efficiency and equity in the use
of public funds. To this end, i) a single structure will be created to handle assignments, ii)
criteria will be established to identify schools that will receive new staff, in order to
significantly improve staffing consistency. This will be based partly on the establishment of
criteria for assignments to the Willayas (administrative districts), in order to reduce current
regional disparities, and partly on criteria for assignments to schools within the different
Willayas. Finally, decisions will be taken to ensure that the assignment and movement of
personnel are concentrated between the two entry periods and not during the school year. An
incentives system will be set up to encourage teachers who work in a difficult context or who
provide a specific service (multigrade teaching, etc.).
2.2 Pedagogical Management
Apart from the pedagogical aspects and the quality of teaching that are the subject of specific
actions, measures will be taken to ensure that the resources mobilized in each institution of
leaming indeed translate into tangible results among the students (level of learning,
successfully passing national examinations, etc.). In this regard, it is decided i) that both the
institutions' resources and student results will be evaluated on a regular basis, ii) that this
information will be released to all the stakeholders and partners involved in schools, iii) that
inspectors will be asked to target their actions so that the weakest institutions do indeed
improve, and iv) that sanctions will be taken, if necessary.
2.3 Administrative and Financial Management
The strengthening of budgetary and material management will be based on a redefinition of
annual budget preparation procedures and the gradual implementation of budgets/programs
that are better adapted to multi-year development projects and allow for a greater
rationalization of investments and equipment. Efforts will also be made to improve the
management and maintenance of buildings, furniture, school materials and textbooks, to ensure
that they are functional over desirable periods of time. To accompany the current shift toward
decentralizing the MEN's services, regional national education boards (DREN) will be
established to bring the system's admninistration closer to the users. The missions of these
boards will be defined and adapted resources to help them fulfill these missions will be
- 114-



provided. Likewise, management committees will be created in the schools to provide a forum
for teachers and parents to discuss the institution's projects.
III- Management and Supervision of the Program.
Within the framework of the implementation of the National Education Sector Development Program, the
execution of the Program is put under the responsibility of the Ministry of Education, which defines the
broad orientations of the planning, supervision, evaluation and coordination of the overall program at the
central and regional levels. It is particularly responsible for:
*  the development of an expenditures framework in accordance with the program objectives, the
commitments towards the financial donors, the Government's budget instructions and the level of
participation of the external partners;
* the development of annual action plans and budgets for the various program components;
* the implementation of the parts of the annual action plans and budgets which are under the
responsibility of the Ministry of Education, and the coordination of the parts which are under the
responsibility of other ministries associated with the Program.
* the coordination of relations with donors as regards the preparation of the program and the progress of
its implementation;
 the centralization and consolidation of the quarterly progress reports of the Program execution, which
are written by the staff responsible of the components;
*  preparation of the sessions of the Program Coordination Comnittee.
Moreover, the Government plans to entrust the Direction des Projets Education Formation (DPEF) of the
Ministry of Development and Economic Affairs with the management of the Program's financial resources,
until the reinforcement of the institutional capacity of the Ministry of Education, planned under the
Program, is completed. To that effect, the DPEF will be specifically in charge of the following:
- prepare the annual financial reports of the Program, in accordance with internationally accepted
accounting standards, and take the necessary measures for the auditing of these reports;
* ensure that disbursements are done in accordance with the requirements made by the Program partners;
*  manage the material resources allocated to the preparation and coordination of the Program;
* provide the different institutions in charge of the execution of the program with the necessary support,
and, in particular, with information, training and consulting services;
* manage the funds and ensure the regular supervision of commitments and payments;
* ensure strict observance of the procedures of the various partners involved in the Program;
* collect the results of the works undertaken and the audits performed within the framework of the
Program and submit them to the Program's Management Committee and Coordination Committee.
- 115-



IV- Specific Questions
1) Girls'education
The Government will develop special measures to encourage the education and retention of girls in the
system by reducing the handicaps they face in their education as well as in examinations.
*   strengthening female education in primary schools in regions with strong gender disparities;
*   Establishing incentive policies to increase the presence of girls in secondary schools and other higher
institutions.
2) Promotion of the private sector
In an effort to make universal education a reality and to ensure quality training, the Government
will support the development of the private sector at all levels of the educational system through a
set of incentives and institutional measures, including:
o   creating and implementing an administrative structure at the MEN responsible for the accreditation,
management and control of private education;
*   instituting incentive measures for the development of the private sector through the establishment of a
private-sector promotion support fund;
*   setting out specifications for private sector intervention;
=   establishing educational support for teacher training;
-   Establishing a permanent evaluation and infornnation system.
3) School health, environment, and citizenship
The Govenmuent will strengthen its partnerships with the health and environmental sectors to ensure that all
students, irrespective of their educational level, are familiar with the major issues surrounding their civic
and social integration and health promotion. This policy will be based on the following actions:
*   adapting and strengthening programs concerning the improvement of school health, environmental
protection and citizenship;
*   adapting training modules for student teachers in school health, environmental protection and civic
education;
*   establishing a health, hygiene and nutrition policy in educational institutions;
*   installation of latrines (for teachers, boys and girls) and access to drinking water for all schools;
- 116-



* In-service training, production and distribution of educational material.
4) Early childhood development
Aware of the importance of preschool education in preparing children for a revamped primary education
program, the Government will provide all its support to help develop this sector by encouraging the
establishment of partnerships involving families, local communities and local and foreign NGOs, as well as
technical and financial partnerships. Actions to be taken during the decade will focus on:
*   Support for the development of community initiatives;
* Early childhood development training system;
* Bolstering of early childhood resource centers;
* Development of institutional management capacities.
5) Non-formal religious education
With respect to non-fonnal religious education, the Government will take steps to ensure more
complementarity between formal schools and Mahadras so that the latter can better fulfill their role in the
national universal education effort.
This policy will involve:
   conducting a study for the identification and classification of the Mahadras;
v   establishing specifications with minimum conditions of openness and exercise;
* establishing a qualification system for the Mahadras, to make them eligible for financial and
pedagogical support from the Government;
= strengthening incentive measures for the sector's development;
* reinforcing supervision and control mechanisms;
*   improving coordination between the Department of National Education and the SEAEO in terms of
planning, pedagogical programs and training of Sheiks;
* Establishing linkages between qualified Mahadras, on the one hand, and vocational training centers and
the educational system, on the other.
6) Literacy
A specific national policy will be drawn up for the eradication of illiteracy and resources will be allocated
to allow the target populations to escape the cycle of poverty. Literacy, in this instance, will be functional,
in that it will be focused on income-generating activities and trades, or will have a social and civic function
in general, thereby allowing people to use the language skills acquired in their daily lives.
- 117 -



The development of this policy will entail:
*   identifying target populations using the general census results;
*   rallying all stakeholders involved in the fight against illiteracy;
*   diversifying intervention tools (involvement of the Mahadras, experimentation with mobile literacy
units, consolidation of literacy campaigns and improvement of the performance of permanent literacy
centers);
*   controlling and developing literacy engineering;
*   Improving communication assistance.
The ten-year strategy for the development of the educational sector outlined above is supported by a
ten-year program, the ESDP. This program constitutes the framework around which all actions in the
educational sector will be built.

Prime Minister, Minister of National Education,
Mahfoudh Ould Mohamed Aly (sgd)

Additional
Annex 12
Macro-Financial Framework of ESDP
MAURITANIA: Education Sector Development Program
Unofficial Translation
Developing a basic education system comprising primary education and lower secondary education, for a
total of 10 years of schooling beginning in 2007, is a priority underpinning all the orientations proposed
under the ten-year program that is being prepared. The educational policy can be oriented from a systemic
perspective based on the following points:
Consolidation of the system's management through the development of forecasting capabilities,
structuring of the central administration, permanent evaluation of the system, and programming of
recruitment.
*     Improvement of educational provision by establishing a school map, reorganizing the university
and technical and vocational training.
Enhancement of the quality of teaching by renovating programs and educational material,
professionalizing and structuring initial and in-service training, and improving pedagogical and
administrative guidance.
*     Improvement of human resources management by establishing career tracking based on merit and
performance appraisal and the rationalization of assignments.
*     Elimination of regional disparities by implementing a voluntary resource allocation policy.
*     Promotion of girls' enrollment, which remains a major component of Govemment policy.
*     Expansion of the role of the private sector in the provision of education through a private-sector
promotion policy.
The overarching goal of the simulations was to improve quality at all levels and relevance vis-a-vis the
labor market at the highest levels (higher, technical and vocational). These simulations were also conducted
with the idea that there were strong educational policy decisions concerning major segments of the system,
on the one hand, and less defined options for other segments, on the other hand. Primary, higher and upper
secondary education fall into the first category, while technical, vocational and lower secondary education
fall into the second category. The strategy of conducting simulations was then to quantify the defined
policies, taking into account both their quantitative and qualitative dimensions, and to propose altematives
for the quantitative and qualitative development of technical, vocational and lower secondary education.
Apart from quantifying the different elements conceming the resources to be used, simulations were also
conducted for resources likely to be used, with a focus on the balance to be achieved between jobs and the
resources of the educational sector as a whole.
The overall macro-economic framework chosen for these simulations is built around the following
scenarios: average annual growth of 6% over the period, tax revenues representing about 27.8% and an
increase in regular education spending as a percentage of Govemment revenues, from 13.5% to 16.5% at
- 119-



the end of the period.
The purpose of this note is to present the simulations for the different levels of education in order to
determine their impact on the education operating budget and to help decision-makers to make the
appropriate choices.
I. Primary Education
The proposed policy revolves around five main ideas:
1.     Quickly achieving universal access in first grade of primary school; this goal is expected to be
reached by 2005;
2.     Substantially improving the retention rate in the primary education, mainly by ensuring educational
continuity over the six years of schooling; the goal is to gradually raise the percentage of students who start
and complete the primary education from 55% today to about 78% in 2010;
3.     Reducing class repetitions. The repetition rates currently stand at 14% for the first five years of the
cycle and at 30% for the final year. These rates will be gradually reduced to 5% between grades 1 and 5
and to 15% in grade 6;
4.     Improving the quality of teaching. The first measure concems the introduction of bilingualism,
which will require strong actions: i) ensuring that ENI graduates are effectively bilingual and ii) recycling a
sufficient proportion of teachers currently in service. Likewise, from a more logistical standpoint, three
major aspects will be considered: a) establishment of salary premiums for teachers in hardship areas
(estimated at 15% of salary for about 35% of potentially eligible teachers, on the understanding that the
awarding criteria are still to be determined); b) gradual reduction of the student-teacher ratio from 48 today
to 40 in 2010. This will help develop education (retention) in rural areas where school populations are low
and where classes (even using the multigrade formula) will have reduced enrollments. It will also help
reduce congestion in certain urban classes where enrollment currently exceeds the pedagogically desirable
threshold; c) provision of sufficient resources for the different activities needed for the qualitative operation
and better management of primnary education (administration, evaluation and pedagogical management,
in-service training of teachers, school manuals and didactic/pedagogic materials for students and teachers).
In the current situation, these expenditures represent only 17.5% of the teacher wage bill, a percentage that
is expected to increase gradually to 31% by 2010.
5.    Improving administrative and pedagogical management activities. They are mentioned here solely
for the record, since they only involve relatively modest specific budgetary resources, which are
incorporated into point c) above. First of all, these activities are designed to better manage the allocation of
staff and resources to the schools. It would be necessary to: i) define the institutional framework in which
the responsibilities of the centralized (DEF, DP, DPC) and decentralized (DREF, local authorities) levels
will be clarified, and ii) define and apply homogenous and transparent criteria for the allocation of staff to
the schools. Secondly, these activities are meant to improve pedagogical management, not so much as
defined by conventional actions (inspection, school management), but rather in terms of the expected
functionality of the mechanism. What is at issue here is the efficient management (measurement and
regulation) of the transfornation between public resources used at the schools and outcomes (student
results in national examinations and/or assessment tests).
As for the wage policy outlook, apart from the establishment of salary premiums for teachers in hardship
- 120-



areas mentioned above, the average level of salary for teachers is expected to increase by 24% by 2010, in
constant monetary units, but at a slightly slower pace than the projected per capita GDP. As a result, the
salary level would represent about 4.5 times the per capita GDP. It should be noted that, in the current
situation, the average level of teacher salary represents about 5.1 times the per capita GDP.
11. Lower Secondary Education
The proposed policy is to gradually ensure continuity between the primary level and the lower secondary
level to ultimately provide 10 years of basic education. In this regard, the plan for the decade is to establish
measures that facilitate pedagogical linkages between primary education and the lower secondary level to
better prepare graduates for entry into the labor force. The main measures proposed revolve around the
following points:
Considering the Improvement of the retention rate at the primary level, the plan for the next decade is to
maintain the transition rate between primary and secondary education at 50%. This measure will help
increase the number of students accessing secondary education from 18,344 students to about 35,000 in
2010, for an overall student enrollment in this cycle of 92,803 (versus roughly 39,000 at present). The
scenario of maintaining the transition rate at 50% was preferred over that of raising the rate to 70%.
Indeed, the latter scenario will increase new admissions from 18,344 to 48,569, for an overall
enrollment of 125,584, which would seem impossible to achieve simply from a logistical standpoint, not
to mention the difficulties that would be involved in financing this option.
Improving the quality of this level of education is a major component of the educational policy that will
be followed. In this regard, three types of measures are considered concurrently: i) gradual but
substantial reduction of the student-teacher ratio from the current level of 36 to 31 (representing an
average class size of 40 students) in 2010; ii) adaptation of teaching programs to better prepare
graduates for entry into the labor force (accounting, etc.); iii) stabilizing non-salary expenditures
(administration, inspection and evaluation, pedagogical materials for teachers and students, in-service
training for teachers), which today represent about 85% of the teacher wage bill (17,676 UM/student in
2010 versus 12,430 UM/student in 1998 in constant monetary units).
As for the wage policy outlook, the average level of teacher salary is expected to rise by 22%, in
constant monetary units, but at a slightly slower pace than the projected per capita GDP. As a result,
the salary level will represent about 6.1 times the per capita GDP. It should be noted that, in the current
situation, the average teacher salary represents about 7.1 times the per capita GDP.
Furthermore, the third year is expected to be a skills certification year leading to several options: i) entry
into the labor force, or ii) entry into technical and vocational schools, and iii) entry into year 4, which will
be considered an orientation year toward the second cycle with no repetition allowed.
III. Upper Secondary Education
The overall outlook for the educational policy followed for the second cycle of secondary education is to
prepare access to higher education in terms of both quantity and quality. In this context, the basic
regulation of the flow of students with access to higher education is expected to be facilitated by that
introduced for access to the second cycle of secondary education. The following educational policy
measures are therefore envisaged:
1.    While the current number of students entering the general second cycle of secondary education is
- 121 -



slightly more than 8,000, this number is expected to decline gradually to between 5,000 and 5,500; this
goal will be achieved by 2005, and it will help stabilize student enrollment for this level at about 14,500;
2.     The significant improvement of the quality of this level of education is a key component of the
educational policy that will be followed. In this regard, three types of measures are considered
concurrently: i) gradual but substantial reduction of the student-teacher ratio from the current level of 23 to
16 (representing an average class size of 28 students) in 2010; ii) restructuring of the educational streams
by allocating 50% of spaces to science education and by putting more emphasis on mathematics, physics
and chemistry; iii) expanded use of resources per student in terms of both investment spending (installation
of functional computer equipment, science laboratories and documentation services) and operations. On the
latter point, it is anticipated that non-salary expenditures (administration, inspection and evaluation,
pedagogic material for teachers and students, in-service training for teachers), which today represent about
45% of the teacher wage bill (12,478 MU/student in 1998), will increase gradually to 70% in 2010 (40,280
MU/student in constant MU).
IV. Technical and Vocational Training (TVT)
The policy orientation for the next decade with respect to TVT marks a shift from a supply-driven system
to a demand-driven system. Improving the quality of training will constitute a strategic objective for the
decade. Through its job-oriented focus, this component of the mechanism must play a major role in
regulating flows within the system.
Due to the uncertainties and constraints faced by TVT, the 10-year strategy will be phased in through the
development of pilot projects aimed at quality improvement, development and diversification of supply, and
introduction of new training modes (schcol-to-job and apprenticeship schemes) that are more reflective of
the needs of the productive sectors.
The implementation of this policy will be in two phases:
Phase 1: experimentation with new training modes through the development of pilot projects, establishment
of structures for the coordination, engineering, financing, monitoring and systematic evaluation of related
activities. This first five-year phase should help double TVT enrollments (currently 2,500).
The implementation of this first phase of the strategy involves the following major activities:
1.    Improvement of the quality of existing training programs by introducing school-to-job schemes and
in-service training for staff. For TVT under the responsibility of the MEN, this improvement will be
achieved gradually between 2003 and 2005 (at a rate of 25% of current enrollments per year), in order to
cover all enrollments in the current sections over these four years. As for vocational training under the
responsibility of the Ministry of Public Service, school-to-job schemes will be introduced simultaneously
and will cover all enrollments beginning in 2003. School-to-job schemes will be implemented at a rate of
20% of in-service training time.
2.     Creation of an additional admission capacity of 1,470 places for school-to-job training schemes
lasting one to two years, between 2002 and 2005.
3.     Creation of an apprenticeship training capacity of 1,200 places between 2002 and 2005.
Apprenticeship training will last no more than one year and will be given at a rate of 50% of in-service
training time. The training will cover trades in which the economy is already primed for this type of
- 122-



training.
4.    In addition to the pennanent admission capacities, in school-to-job and apprenticeship schemes, an
Autonomous Fund for the Promotion of TVT is expected to be set up, to encourage demand-driven
management of TVT through the development of new training modes. This Fund will allow companies to
develop short-term training schemes (not exceeding 6 months) to meet their qualification needs. A
lump-sum is to be earmarked for the launching of the Fund.
5.    Apart from these activities, the strategy also includes a segment to improve the quality and
relevance of TVT via the creation of the National Institute for the Promotion of TVT, which will be
responsible for the system's engineering through: (i) analysis and processing of data on the labor market's
qualification needs, (ii) establishment of a quality system, (iii) renovation of training programs, (iv)
development of specific engineering for new training modes (school-to-job and apprenticeship schemes), (v)
establishment of a system to monitor and evaluate TVT activities, (vi) TVT promotion activities.
Phase 2: At the end of the fifth year of the program, an overall evaluation of the experimental first phase
will be conducted. Based on the results of this evaluation, the necessary corrections will be made for the
development of the TVT mechanism and its adaptation to demand. Regardless of the volume of
development that will be prescribed following the evaluation of phase I of the program, the resulting
simulations are based on the assumption, for example, that enrollments will double for phase 2.
V. Higher Education
The higher education policy is designed, broadly speaking, to make higher education congruent with the
qualitative and quantitative needs of the national economy. This general goal is based on the following
scenarios:
1.    Enrollments, which have ballooned to about 9,500 students today, are expected to be reduced
gradually to about 7,500 by 2005; this will lead to a capping of the number of new admissions at about
2,600 per year.
2.    As regards quality improvement, the following measures will be considered together: i) gradual
reduction of the student-teacher ratio from 31 to 14 in 2010, in keeping with intemational standards
conceming vocational training; ii) the percentage of students in the science and technical streams will
increase from 10% to 25% in 2005; iii) increase in operating expenses per student from 22,000 UM to
50,000 UM in 2010.
3.    Graduat 40% reduction of the quota for foreign scholarships by 2005 and the reallocation of these
amounts for the financing of new training, notably vocational and short-term training given by national
institutions.
4.    Creation of a scientific research support fund of about 160 million UM in 2010 accompanied by a
resulting investment in specialized equipment and documentation.
- 123-



The establishment of these measures is based on the assumption that regular public expenditures on
education as a percentage of GDP will increase from 3.7% to about 4.7% in 2010, while the resources
anticipated in the simulations will represent about 4.6% of GDP. To achieve a balance in regular
expenditures, the share of education expenses in total Goverrmnent revenues should increase from 13.5% to
15.5%.
If expenses related to vocational training (MFPTJS) are included, they will represent 4.9% of GDP and
16.8% of Government revenues at the end of the period.

Additional
Annex 13
Education Sector Reform in Mauritania
MAURITANIA: Education Sector Development Program
1. The Context
Over the past three decades Mauritania's education system has evolved significantly, passing through
several reforms (1967, 1973, 1979) aimed at establishing a system that responds to the socio-cultural and
socio-economic developments needs of the country. The penultimate reform (1979) lasted for twenty years
during which there was a dual education system separated by two languages of instruction (Arabic and
French). The system was characterized by high enrolment ratios which had increased remarkably from 54%
in 1985 to 90% in 1998 but quality was poor as indicated by poor pupil performance and retention.
Educational provision was costly because of the multiple human and material resources adapted to a dual
system of education. Poor mastery of the languages was identified as one of the main reasons for weak
pupil performance particularly in science subjects and the content of school curricula was allegedly not
adapted to the economic needs of the country. The necessity to improve the performance, efficiency and
competitiveness of the education system at a global level increased the pressure for the Government to
urgently reform the system.
2. The Reform
Through law number 99-012 of 26 April, 1999, the Government of Mauritania (GOM) decided to
undertake a broad based reform aimed at correcting the weaknesses of the education system. Although the
reform affects all levels of the education sector, the focus has been on the primary and secondary levels.
The 1999 education reform seeks to achieve three main objectives which are:-
(i)    establishing a unified bilingual system of education where Arabic is the language of instruction for
the literary subjects and French is the language of instruction for mathematics and science subjects,
(ii)   increasing the duration of the first cycle of secondary education from three to four years
(iii)  introducing English language, information technology and civic instruction from the first year of
secondary education.
Primary education measures:
Conserve Arabic as the medium of instruction to all children in grade one in the public or private
sector.
Introduce French from the second year of primary, teaching it for 6 hours a week
Introduce the teaching of Mathematics in French from grade three of primary
Introduce the teaching of natural sciences in French in grades five and six of primary.
Introduce civic education to be taught independently of religious education
Due to the small capacity of secondary schools to receive the majority of students from primary to
secondary, vocational training centers would be opened in the different regions to admit some of the
students who have no access into secondary. The possibility of extending the basic cycle to include first
cycle secondary will be examined later as the reform works its way through the system.
First Cycle Secondary measures:
Extend the duration of the cycle from 3 to 4 years
Introduce the teaching of English in the first year for 2 hours per week
Continue the teaching of mathematics and natural sciences in French
Introduce physical sciences in the third year and information technology in the fourth year of the
- 130 -



first cycle,
*      Introduce civic education to be taught independently of religious education as of the first year of
the cycle
*      Revise the number of hours and coefficients to reflect the changes in the level of language usage.
The end of first cycle of secondary education examination (Brevet) will be unified and will comprise of
tests in English and physics with adequate coefficients. At the end of the fourth year regulatory mechanisms
will be put for transition into the second cycle. Students who do not have access to second cycle general
education will take entrance examninations into middle level technical and professional schools. Students
who intend to pursue general education after the first cycle, will have a choice between five disciplines
namely modem letters, Arabic, earth and life sciences, mathematics and technology.
Apart from the simultaneous introduction of civic education in all grades of primary and secondary
education and the introduction of English language in the first year of the first cycle secondary, the reform
is expected to be implemented gradually (year by year) over a period of twelve years. The first cohort of the
reform started is currently in the third grade of primary.
Cognizant of other weaknesses relating to the external efficiency of the education system, the reform also
calls for a complete overhaul of the system. In order to attain the goals of global competitiveness and
poverty reduction, the reform emphasizes the need to attain universal access to basic education, enhance the
quality of education by making schools and classrooms the center of change, improve school curricula and
training programs so as to produce graduates who would respond to the needs of the labor market.
3. The process
The 1999 reform law sparked a range of activities in the education sector and laid the foundation for the
unprecedented development of a strategic education policy framework. Studies were undertaken leading to
the production of a series of reports namely, education sector country status report (primary and
secondary), technical and vocational education report, higher education study report, an audit of the
organizational structure and administrative management of the Ministry of Education. The major issues
identified through these studies include:- limited and inequitable access to primary and secondary education
due mostly to supply constraints, poor quality and low intemal efficiency of primary and secondary
education due to high repetition and dropout rates, low institutional capacity for system management,
minimally responsive technical and vocational education, inefficient higher education, inadequate funding
and sub-optimal allocation of resources particularly at the school level. The Govemment set up a
consultative body to discuss the findings of these studies and develop strategies to resolve the problems
identified. A strategic policy framework documents was prepared after these discussions and the raison
dW'tre of this framework is to provide guidance for the implementation of future activities and interventions
aimed at achieving sector investment goals.
4. The challenges
To achieve the Government's stated education development goals and produce the desired effects, there are
a core set of challenges linked both to the management of the system and pedagogic practices, that the
Government would need to meet. These broadly include language skill development for sufficient quantities
of teachers, finance and resource allocation, educational management and information, quality, efficiency
as well as equity issues. These areas are complementary hence the effective implementation of the reform
requires a mix of activities that are consistent and mutually reinforcing from each one of them.
Language skill development.
- 131 -



One of the major requirements for a successful implementation of the reform is the development of the
linguistic capacity of teachers to operate a truly bilingual education system. The present teaching corps
consists mainly of teachers who are proficient either in Arabic or French. The market for those who can be
bilingual is relatively small so the Government would need to attract high school graduates who have had
their secondary general education especially in French and who can communicate in Arabic, into the
teaching profession. The number of sufficiently bilingual teachers in the system is relatively small now
compared to the numbers required. Extra efforts need to be made to provide intensive language training to
existing teachers who have the potential of becoming bilingual and current pre-service teacher education
programs need to be revised to include language skills training. In-service teacher courses must also be
organized to strengthen the capacity of teachers in the schools.
Finance and resource allocation
The GOM faces the challenge of providing adequate financing for education. Constraints on public
resources have limited the ability of the Government to allocate sufficient resources to education. The share
of GDP that is allocated to education decreased from 5.2% in 1985 to 3.5% in 1998. The need to finance
activities of the reform as well as other educational services cannot be overemphasized. However within the
competing claims on its limited resources, the Government has to make decisions about the allocations of
national resources, aid and proceeds of debt relief to key sectors like education. Decisions on the
appropriate level of overall spending for education and a determination of how much should be allocated to
each level is crucial. Within each level, the allocation of resources would have to be made to activities that
have the highest marginal returns. The challenge is not just how much funding is allocated to education but
how efficiently and how effectively the resources are used. There should be sufficient information about the
system as well as local capacity to undertake cost/benefit analysis of alternative forms of investments in
order to optimize resource utilization. Sources of finance, and a definition of the degree of contribution
from each needs to be carefully thought through. Another challenge is for the Government of Mauritania to
encourage other service providers such as the private sector (including non-Governmental organizations) to
play the role of provider of services and or sources of finance.
Educational manazement.
Effective implementation of the new policy directions identified within the context of the reform would
require appropriate management competences within the Ministry of Education. Experience from other
countries has shown that resource use can be significantly improved by changing the management strategy
of the sector. The most frequently considered option focuses on the centralization versus the
decentralization of educational service provision. Generally it is believed that the efficiency of educational
spending increases when the decision points are closer to the schools or decentralized. The current system
of management in Mauritania is centralized although there are regional services which often rely on the
central level for management decisions. The Government's challenge is to balance decentralization of
decision making to local levels while making sure that the central level oversees the quality and coherence
of the entire system. It is important to note that decentralization is not a panacea to the problems of
education systems. While there are merits in decentralizing educational services, the options cover a range
of shades from white to black. The Government of Mauritania would have to choose the degree of
decentralization that it is comfortable with. Irrespective of what option is chosen, a critical concept for the
improvement of efficiency is school autonomy. Greater autonomy of the school and better links between the
school and the community hold great potential for the improvement of opportunities for learning. In an
autonomous school situation, teachers and school directors are better placed to find solutions and take
responsibility for improving teaching and the performance of pupils regarding what is taught.
- 132 -



Qualitv and efficiency
One of the central aims of the reform is to improve the opportunity for children to learn. Universal primary
education will not be achieved nor maintained without sufficient attention paid to improving the quality of
education provided in the schools. Quality improvement results in reduced repetition, greater efficiency and
affordability. The challenge is for the GOM to move from a narnow focus of academic performance to an
emphasis on learning outcomes for all children. This calls for a more holistic approach where the needs of
the learner, within the classroom and the school are looked at carefully. A set of necessary conditions that
flow from this approach include:- developing committed and motivated teachers, defining and implementing
appropriate curricula, providing appropriate teaching and learning materials, using appropriate languages
for teaching and learning, promoting community participation in school activities, managing physical assets
effectively, strengthening school based management, undertaking meaningful assessments and using the
results as feedback to enhance teaching, creating a child-friendly learning environment, improving the
supervision of schools, increasing learning time at school and harnessing new and old technologies.
Equity focus.
The attainment of poverty reduction goals requires universal access to primary education. Currently, there
are great disparities in educational opportunities for children from different socio-economic backgrounds
and for those living in urban and rural areas. Mauritania has high enrolment ratios (close to 90%) but
retention is low (55%) partly because of poor quality and a shortfall of schools classrooms especially in
remote rural and peri-urban areas. The challenge for the Government is to enable all children to have
access to primary education by constructing sufficient numbers of classrooms, rehabilitating deteriorating
physical and using existing structures more efficiently through double shifting and multi-grading. It is also
important to ensure that education provided is of good quality. Gender equality in schooling is also a
challenge that needs to be addressed. Educational opportunities for girls can be made equal to that of boys
by ensuring that there are more female teachers in the system, that schools are well equipped to cater to
girls' needs, and that teachers are trained to be gender sensitive. Expanding pre-school education is another
challenge that will improve children's readiness to learn when they begin primary school. Currently
pre-schools exist in urban centers and this creates a disadvantage for rural who tend to repeat grade one
partly because compared to urban children, they have received fewer if any experiences at home that are
conducive to cognitive development.
Institutional Capacity strengthening.
The challenge to strengthen institutional capacity in the Ministry of education from the central through the
regional and district to the school level is urgent. The current limited capacity to budget, and to utilize
resources more effectively and with accountability further undermines the Ministry's capacity to manage
the sector. Without sufficient capacity to plan, manage and maintain the education sector, the Government
of Mauritania will find it difficult to attain the goal to provide education of good quality. Technical
capacity, including the development of appropriately trained education specialists, economists and
statisticians who can research and analyze sector issues is crucial and needs to be encouraged.
Strengthening existing capacity and creating new capacity to realize and sustain the new vision is essential.
Donor coordination.
The Government of Mauritania receives significant support from external development partners in forms
that vary from financial resources to knowledge sharing. However, past efforts made within the context of
education projects by various development agencies have remained largely uncoordinated and often
overlap. The challenge is for the Govermment of Mauritania to lead and organize all donors to work in
partnership amongst themselves and with the Government towards achieving the objectives of the reform.
- 133 -



Additional
Annex 14
Key Elements of the Quality Enhancement Strategy
MAURITANIA: Education Sector Development Program
The key elements of the government of Mauritania strategy to improve the quality of education are: (i)
improving the efficiency of pre-service and in-service teacher training, (ii) improving the management of
the teaching and learning process, (iii) improving the deployment of teaching staff. The following note
summarizes the issues and policies involved and the actions that are planned with regard to quality
enhancement..
I - Improving teacher training
Findings on the quality of primary education (IPN studies on program evaluations and on the performance
of 4th and 6th year students) underscore the low quality of education in Mauritania. Students only acquire
approximately 40% of the content of targeted programs, with performance varying widely among the
various classes within the sample - a variation which remains significant even when factors like the impact
of students' social characteristics and mobilized resources are controlled for.
The ESDP faces considerable challenges particularly as, in the near future, the education system itself will
be transformed and consolidated into a single bilingual system with modified programs and the introduction
of civic and health education.
The recent evaluations show that of the 8000 teachers in the system: (i) 1,200 are already bilingual, (ii)
2,400 are considered almost bilingual (a refresher course can be offered in the short term), and (iii) 1,600
seem to have a long way to go to acquire the desired skills (however, a large proportion of teachers in this
category could acquire the necessary skills by participating in a more robust training program). Finally,
the remaining 2,800 primary school teachers (essentially Arabic speaking) present a more difficult
scenario, as it does not seem likely that they can become bilingual. Apart from the language issue, these
evaluations further indicate that though a majority of the teachers have reasonable knowledge of the
curriculum content, not all do; thus activities designed to improve subject matter skills as well as
pedagogical skills will be required for aproportion of the teachers. Therefore, a double strategy needs to be
implemented to authorize the effective introduction of the reform concerning bilingualism and reinforce the
skills of some of the current teaching staff.
First of all, on the issue of bilingualism, from now on all new recruits (approximately 2500 from now to
2005), will receive initial training at the ENI (teachers' training college) which will qualify them for
bilingualism. Besides, all teachers do not have to be bilingual (particularly in the short term) for the reform
to be effectively implemented because: (i) first grade studies will be conducted in the Arabic language; and
(ii) second to sixth year elementary school programs will be taught in both languages. The plan therefore is
to: (i) implement training sessions specifically for the 2400 elementary school teachers who are potentially
bilingual; and (ii) organize the posting of teachers so as to offer all students curriculum content in the
languages stipulated in the context of the reform. This will be done through a priority posting of bilingual
teachers to smaller schools, where much flexibility is needed from teachers to cover the curricula. A policy
based on meaningful salary incentives has been proposed to facilitate this reposting.
Plans for providing language training to the 2400 teachers who are almost bilingual include the use of
training modules, which are currently being developed (with the assistance of the Cooperation franqaise
- 134-



(French Cooperation Agency) which will also train the trainers), and training sessions which will be
provide by the district and regional capitals during school holidays (two weeks in December, two weeks in
March, and two months in the sumrner). Apart from these training periods, activities will also be planned
during the school year (plans are underway for distance education supported by the use of radios). At the
end of the first year of training, the teachers will be able to take a test to obtain a certificate of bilingualism;
those unable to become certified will have to pursue a longer training course.
Finally, with regard to the 1600 teachers who are expected to require longer training to be able to teach in
the two languages, training in their weaker language will be provided from academic year 2002-2003, with
the knowledge that it may take a few years for them to obtain the required certification.
With regard to the need to irmprove the subject matter knowledge and pedagogical skills of some of the
teachers, and the need to train all teachers in new subject areas as well as in the methodology of the new
teaching program (targeting skills, classroom interactions, management of multigrade classes), important
activities have been planned. A triple strategy will be implemented. Any traditional training offered by the
two EN), ENS and IPN will focus mainly on the training of trainers, inspectors (160 inspectors) and
inspection officers. In fact, the main pedagogical strategy will be implemented at the local level, through
consolidation around the districts, and through the actions taken in the schools with respect to the school
directors and individual teachers. Thus, the training of school directors will in particular be oriented, on the
one hand, towards the management of the school project in partnership with the parent/teacher associations
and, on the other hand, towards providing support to teachers in their schools who have shortcomings and
are taking refresher courses.
The recruitment of personnel for inspections as well as for the budget, for the recruitrnent effort as well as
for the inspectors' operations in the schools in their districts, will be included in the MENbudget.
Issues Concerning the Training of Secondary Education Teachers and Staff. Important aspects of the
training of teachers and staff at the secondary education level include: (i) the substantial quantitative
extension of the system planned in the 10-year program; (ii) the implementation of new curriculum content
currently being developed (new subjects and new teaching methodologies); and (iii) the provision of
language training to several science teachers and teachers of some literary disciplines.
With regard to the first two points, initial training will be offered at ENS according to the new
methodology, which, on the one hand, takes into consideration the content of the new secondary education
programs and, on the other hand, reinforces the professional and pedagogical dimension compared to
current practices. The details about this training are under development. Some of the modules developed by
the ENS for the initial training will be used as the basis for the continuous training of current teachers.
With regard to retraining some of the teachers in the languages of instruction stipulated by the reform, an
assessment of language skills comparable to the one conducted at the primary school level (see above) will
be used to evaluate the skills of the teachers concerned. The results of this assessment will indicate: a) the
teachers who have the appropriate language skills, b) are capable of obtaining certification through
relatively simple upgrading activities, and c) those who require stronger actions. For those in category a),
language training will be offered during vacations (following guidelines identical to those used at the
primary school level), whereas for those in category c), a full year of training will be provided at the Centre
d 'Enseignement des Langues Secondes (second language teaching center) at the Universite de Nouakchott.
II - Improving the Management of the Learning and Teaching process
- 135 -



Intemational experience suggests that improving educational management requires that the interests of all
the individual actors in the education system are aligned with the interests of the overall system. The
education system clearly has an interest in attaining the goals defined in the content of the curriculum and
ensuring that the resources mobilized for education produce the maximum learning outcomes for pupils.
Currently, the individual actors in the education system in Mauritania are: (i) not adequately informed
about the precise content and goals of the curriculum. Textbooks and teachers' guides are not sufficiently
available; and/or (ii) teachers not sufficiently motivated to channel their energies and skills to achieve the
task entrusted to them. New structures accompanied with formnal contracts that define precisely what is
expected of the particular actor concerned (teachers, school principals, school inspectors, DREF,
education administrators at the center) are being developed. The standard structure is organized around
three complementary components: (i) definition of what is expected of each actor, (ii) definition of how to
verify what is achieved by each actor; (iii) definition of the consequences of good or bad performance.
Teachers are undoubtedly the principal actors. They must be informed clearly about the content of the
curriculum they must transmit to pupils. They must be provided textbooks and guides. Their
responsibilities for the most important aspects of classroom practices and behavior (attendance, preparation
of lesson plans, planning teaching for the school year, pupil evaluation and remedial work) must be
clarified. They must be informed about how their performance and behavior in transmitting the desired
curricular content will be verified. Finally they must be informed about the personal consequences of their
professional performance (rewards, disciplinary action, professional assistance).
Other actors also have an important role to play in this process: (i) to monitor teacher attendance, an
attendance register could be kept and filled out by both the school principal and representatives of
parent-teacher associations; (ii) to monitor instructional practices such as preparation of lessons, planning
of instructional time, student evaluation, and interaction with parents, teachers should keep a docunent
describing these activities for the entire school year. The principal could monitor the progress of these
activities, as well as the inspector during his/her visits; (iii) to monitor pupil learning, inspectors will play
the main role. They would use examination results and pupil evaluations prepared by IPN to compile an
annual report on the status of each school and its performance, which would be sent to the central
education administration. The report would also describe the actions implemented by the inspectorate to
remedy inadequate practices and performance. Finally, the report would provide instructions for
appropriate rewards and disciplinary actions. Central education administration would establish mechanisms
for training and monitoring of the network of inspectors to help them carry out their functions. Central
administration would also adapt, verify, and apply as necessary, the administrative sanctions of the annual
report of inspectors. Of course, these are only possible measures that should be explored to test their
relevance to the context in Mauritania.
Action Plan
There are four interrelated steps:
i)     Establish a technical team to make concrete proposals and prepare guidelines to ensure that the
proposals are relevant and are implemented effectively in the field. Coordinated by the Inspector General
for Secondary and Technical Education (IGEST), the team would be composed of representatives of IPN,
the Inspectorate for Basic Education (IEF), the Directorate of Basic Education (DEF), the Directorate of
Secondary Education (DES), the Directorate of Planning and Cooperation (DPC), and the Directorate for
Education Projects (DPEF). It would also contain two education officials in the field representing the
Regional Directorate of Basic Education (DREF) and the Teachers Training Institute (Ecole Normale
d 'Instituteurs, ENI). The team would meet as often as necessary under the supervision of IGEST and will
- 136-



present the first draft of its conclusions on September 15, 2001.
ii)    Agree and finalize a regulatory document that defines the new structure for the management and
allocation of educational staff and the responsibilities of the various actors (teachers, school principals,
inspectors, DREF, and central education administration).
iii)    Set up these arrangements through information campaigns-distribution of the new measures to
education officials and to the general public.
iv)    Implement the measures selected for a trial period of three years, at the end of which, an evaluation
will be carried out to determine if adjustments and modifications are needed.
HI - Improving the Deployment of Staff
Deployment of educational staff would be viewed as a two-stage process:
* The first stage is to develop school mapping in the largest sense of the term. This includes the
geographical distribution and educational structure of schools, the number of pupils, and the number of
educational divisions, ways of grouping pupils in basic education, the degree of subject specialization
of teachers in secondary education, particularly in lower secondary school. In this instance, the
education system is faced with contradictory goals. First, schools must be large enough to have
acceptable unit costs, and secondly, schools must be close to the target population, which implies that
they must be small. Use of multigrade teaching in basic education and multiple subject teachers in
secondary education would help reduce the tension between these two objectives. Thus, it important to
clarify, first of all, the guiding principles in this area and to define the school map which will provide
the required number of students in the two educational settings.
One problem that is specific to basic education needs to be underscored. The issue of allocating teachers
must not be viewed on the basis of the number of pupils currently enrolled in as much as a substantial
proportion of basic education schools (82%) do not offer the entire six years of complete basic education.
Thus, it is useful to simulate what would be the number of pupils in a school that offered complete basic
education.
* The second stage of the analysis is to determine what inputs and staff would be appropriate to assign to
schools, taking into account selected possibilities for grouping pupils in basic education (use of
multigrade teaching) and the degree of subject specialization (multipurpose) teachers in secondary
education. The analysis in this note focuses on the second stage.
All countries are faced with the issue of how to distribute educational staff and material inputs. No country
operates optimally in this area, either because the formulas used are not entirely appropriate or because
they are not adequately implemented.
Countries which perform best in allocating staff to schools do so because of the following four reasons:
i)    Assignments and transfers of teachers take place between school years rather than during the
school year. Such transfers are certainly possible but they are the exception rather than the norm.
ii)    If management is decentralized, uniform responsibility and common management principles apply.
- 137 -



iii)    Efficiency criteria are defined to ensure that all educational institutions are funded according to
uniform and equitable formulas. These criteria are simple, transparent, and shielded from extemal
influences.
iv)    Staff positions are assigned to structures (schools and educational institutions, service units)
according to the formulas defined above and are not tied directly to the individuals who occupy them.
These criteria can be used to determine priorities for teacher transfers (seniority, service for a given number
of years in a difficult area, considerations of health, uniting husband and wife, professional performance,
etc.).
In so far as the actual management of staff in basic education is the responsibility of the local level (Walli),
one must ensure that (i) assignments of blocks of positions to different willayas conform to equity and
efficiency criteria and (ii) the mechanism and criteria for assigning positions are well known and applied in
each willaya. These two points will be followed up in item (iv) in the action plan below.
Action Plan
Four complementary stages are proposed:
i)     Establish a technical team to make concrete proposals and prepare guidelines to ensure that the
proposals are relevant and are implemented effectively in the field. Coordinated by the Directorate of
Personnel (DP) the team would be composed of representatives of the Inspectorate for Basic Education
(IEF), ,the Directorate of Basic Education (DEF), the Directorate of Secondary Education (DES), and the
Directorate for Planning and Cooperation (DPC). It would also contain two education officials in the field
representing DREF. The team would meet as often as necessary under the management of DP and would
present the first draft of its conclusions on September 15, 2001.
ii)    Agree and finalize a regulatory document that defines the new structure for managing and
allocating staff and that identifies the responsibilities of the different actors at the central government,
DREF, and Wallis levels.
iii)    Set up these arrangements through information campaigns-distribution of the new arrangements
to education officials and to the general public).
iv)    Implement the arrangements selected for a trial period of three years, at the end of which, an
evaluation will be carried out to detennine if adjustments and modifications are needed.

Primary School Facilities Building Program
MAURITANIA: Education Sector Development Program
(Unofficial Translation)
1.        Current Status
Schools. The number of primary schools increased from 1,214 in 1988/89 to 2,798 in 1999/2000,
averaging 76 new schools per year over the last decade. With only 1% of the schools in 1998/999, private
schools play a very limited role. The pace of school construction has increased from 50 classrooms per
annum, at the beginning of the decade, to more than 300 per annum in 1997/98 but has since declined, as
full coverage is getting nearer.
